reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, image_size, patch_size, num_classes, dim, depth, heads, mlp_dim, channels=3, dropout=0.1, emb_dropout=0.1):
        """
        Vision Transformer (ViT) model.

        :param image_size: The size of the input image (assumed to be square).
        :param patch_size: The size of each patch (assumed to be square).
        :param num_classes: The number of output classes.
        :param dim: The dimensionality of the embedding space.
        :param depth: The number of transformer layers.
        :param heads: The number of attention heads.
        :param mlp_dim: The dimensionality of the MLP (Multi-Layer Perceptron) in the transformer.
        :param channels: The number of channels in the input image (default is 3 for RGB).
        :param dropout: Dropout rate applied in the MLP.
        :param emb_dropout: Dropout rate applied to the embedded patches.
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        
        assert image_size % patch_size == 0, "Image dimensions must be divisible by the patch size."
        num_patches = (image_size // patch_size) ** 2
        patch_dim = channels * patch_size ** 2
        
        self.patch_size = patch_size
        self.pos_embedding = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(1, num_patches + 1, dim))
        self.patch_to_embedding = nn.Linear(patch_dim, dim)
        self.cls_token = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(1, 1, dim))
        self.dropout = nn.Dropout(emb_dropout)
        
        self.transformer = nn.TransformerEncoder(
            nn.TransformerEncoderLayer(d_model=dim, nhead=heads, dim_feedforward=mlp_dim, dropout=dropout),
            num_layers=depth
        )
        
        self.to_cls_token = nn.Identity()
        self.mlp_head = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Linear(dim, mlp_dim),
            nn.GELU(),
            nn.Dropout(dropout),
            nn.Linear(mlp_dim, num_classes)
        )
    
    def forward(self, img):
        """
        Forward pass of the Vision Transformer.

        :param img: The input image tensor, shape (batch_size, channels, image_size, image_size).
        :return: The output tensor, shape (batch_size, num_classes).
        """
        p = self.patch_size
        
        x = img.unfold(2, p, p).unfold(3, p, p).reshape(img.shape[0], -1, p*p*img.shape[1])
        x = self.patch_to_embedding(x)
        
        cls_tokens = self.cls_token.expand(img.shape[0], -1, -1)
        x = torch.cat((cls_tokens, x), dim=1)
        x += self.pos_embedding
        x = self.dropout(x)
        
        x = self.transformer(x)
        
        x = self.to_cls_token(x[:, 0])
        return self.mlp_head(x)

# Test code
image_size = 224
patch_size = 16
num_classes = 10
dim = 512
depth = 6
heads = 8
mlp_dim = 2048
channels = 3
dropout = 0.0
emb_dropout = 0.0

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(2, channels, image_size, image_size)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [image_size, patch_size, num_classes, dim, depth, heads, mlp_dim, channels, dropout, emb_dropout]

If we write a custom kernel per layer that does the entire layer (including matmuls), we replace ~11 launches with 1. That's a big reduction. But implementing a full transformer layer in CUDA is nontrivial but doable. Let's outline the operations per layer: Input: x [B, seq_len, dim] For each layer l: 1. LayerNorm1 over dim: compute mean and variance for each token, then normalize and apply gamma and beta. 2. Compute QKV: linear(x, W_qkv, b_qkv) -> qkv [B, seq_len, 3*dim]. - Alternatively, compute Q, K, V separately. 3. Split into heads: reshape to [B, heads, seq_len, head_dim]. 4. Compute attention scores: Q @ K^T / sqrt(head_dim) -> [B, heads, seq_len, seq_len]. - For each head and each query, compute dot product with all keys. 5. Softmax over keys. 6. Compute attention output: scores @ V -> [B, heads, seq_len, head_dim]. 7. Reshape and project: linear(concat, W_proj, b_proj) -> [B, seq_len, dim]. 8. Dropout1 + residual add: out = x + dropout(attn_out). 9. LayerNorm2. 10. Compute FFN: linear(x, W_fc1, b_fc1) -> [B, seq_len, mlp_dim]. 11. GELU. 12. Dropout2. 13. We can implement a generic linear function inside the kernel: ```cuda __device__ void linear(const float* x, const float* W, const float* b, float* y, int B, int in_dim, int out_dim) { // x: [B][in_dim] // W: [out_dim][in_dim] // b: [out_dim] // y: [B][out_dim] int d = threadIdx.x; if (d >= out_dim) return; for (int b_ = 0; b_ < B; ++b_) { float sum = (b != nullptr) ? b[d] : 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < in_dim; ++k) { sum += x[b_ * in_dim + k] * W[d * in_dim + k]; } y[b_ * out_dim + d] = sum; } } ``` This is simple and correct. The function signature in C++ will be something like: ```cpp void transformer_layer_forward( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor out, torch::Tensor qkv_weight, // shape [dim, 3*dim] torch::Tensor qkv_bias, // [3*dim] torch::Tensor proj_weight, // [dim, dim] torch::Tensor proj_bias, // [dim] torch::Tensor fc1_weight, // [dim, mlp_dim] torch::Tensor fc1_bias, // [mlp_dim] torch::Tensor fc2_weight, // [mlp_dim, dim] torch::Tensor fc2_bias, // [dim] torch::Tensor norm1_weight, // [dim] torch::Tensor norm1_bias, torch::Tensor norm2_weight, torch::Tensor norm2_bias, int batch_size, int seq_len, int dim, int num_heads, int mlp_dim, float dropout_p ); ``` We need to consider the weight layout. We can pre-transpose weights in Python to match the kernel's expected layout. Let's choose `qkv_weight_T` of shape `[dim, 3*dim]`. Actually, if we store as [dim, 3*dim], then in the kernel, thread d reads `qkv_weight_T[k][d]` for its output dim d. That is coalesced if threads have consecutive d. So we pass weights in transposed form. However, PyTorch's default linear weight is [out_features, in_features]. So W_qkv is [3*dim, dim]. We can transpose it to [dim, 3*dim] via `.t().contiguous()`. Similarly for all weights. Now, inside the kernel, we need to handle `blockDim.x`. Since we have many linear layers with different out_dims, using a fixed blockDim based on the largest out_dim is easiest. But if blockDim > out_dim, some threads will be idle. That's okay. For out_dim=512, blockDim=1024 means half the threads idle. That reduces parallelism but for 197 blocks, it's still 197*512 active threads = 100k. That's okay. But for the attention dot product reduction, we need threads to be active. If we have 1024 threads, and we assign heads to groups of 128 threads (since 1024/8=128), but we only need 64 threads per head for head_dim=64. We can still use 128 threads and have each handle part of the dot product? Or we can just use the first 512 threads and have the rest idle during attention, then use all threads for linear layers. But that adds divergence. So maybe it's better to use `blockDim.x = 512` for the transformer kernel. It matches dim=512 and 3*dim=1536 (each thread handles 3 qkv dims). For out_dim=2048 (FFN1), each thread handles 4 dims (2048/512=4). That's fine. We just loop. Let's define `blockDim.x = 512` for transformer kernel. For linear layers: We can write a device function: ```cuda __device__ void linear_forward( const float* x, // [B][in_dim] in shared mem or global const float* W_T, // [in_dim][out_dim] in global const float* bias, // [out_dim] in global float* y, // [B][out_dim] in shared mem int B, int in_dim, int out_dim ) { int d = threadIdx.x; int num_iters = (out_dim + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; for (int iter = 0; iter < num_iters; ++iter) { int out_d = d + iter * blockDim.x; if (out_d >= out_dim) continue; float bval = (bias != nullptr) ? bias[out_d] : 0.0f; for (int b_ = 0; b_ < B; ++b_++) { float sum = bval; for (int k = 0; k < in_dim; ++k) { sum += x[b_ * in_dim + k] * W_T[k * out_dim + out_d]; } y[b_ * out_dim + out_d] = sum; } } } ``` Note: We assume `W_T` is stored as [in_dim, out_dim] row-major. So `W_T[k * out_dim + out_d]` is correct. This uses each thread to compute one or more output dimensions for all batch elements. Since threads read `W_T[k][out_d]` for the same k but different out_d, and out_d are consecutive for consecutive threads, the access is coalesced. For `x`, each thread reads `x[b_][k]` for all k. This is broadcast across threads (same address per thread), which is cached well. Performance: The inner loop over k is a multiply-add with a memory load from global memory for W. This might be memory-bandwidth bound, but since weights are small (e.g., 512*512*4=1MB) and accessed many times, they stay in L2 cache. So it should be okay. Now, shared memory usage: We need buffers for x, qkv, proj, mlp, etc. We can reuse shared memory to minimize usage. Let's design shared memory layout: - `sm_x`: [B][dim] - `sm_tmp1`: [B][max(dim, mlp_dim)] (to store qkv (1536) for B=2, that's 2*1536=3072 floats. mlp_dim=2048, 2*2048=4096. dim=512, 2*512=1024. So max is 4096.) - `sm_tmp2`: [B][max(dim, mlp_dim)] (for attention output, etc) Actually, we can use three buffers: `sm_a`, `sm_b`, `sm_c` each of size `B * max(dim, mlp_dim)`. But we need to be careful with synchronization. Let's allocate shared memory as a single pool and use offsets. ``` __shared__ float sm_pool[3 * 2 * 2048]; // 3 buffers, B=2, mlp_dim=2048. float* sm_x = sm_pool + 0 * B * max_dim; float* sm_tmp1 = sm_pool + 1 * B * max_dim; float* sm_tmp2 = sm_pool + 2 * B * max_dim; ``` Where `max_dim = max(dim, 3*dim, mlp_dim)`. Actually, 3*dim = 1536, mlp_dim = 2048. So max_dim = 2048. But we also need buffers for attention values Q,K,V per head per batch. We can compute QKV into sm_tmp1 (size B * 3*dim = 3072). Then we can layout within sm_tmp1 as q, k, v. For attention: - We need q, k, v for all heads and batch. That's B * heads * head_dim * 3. We have that in sm_tmp1. - We need a buffer for attention output of size B * dim. That's sm_tmp2 (size B*2048, more than enough). - We need a buffer for scores. Since B is small, we can keep scores in registers or shared memory. Let's use a small shared memory array `sm_scores[8][2][2]` for B=2. Actually B is variable. We can allocate `sm_scores[heads][B][B]` but dynamic based on template? Or just use registers per thread. - Each thread can compute its own part of the attention output without storing all scores. Actually, since each head group needs the softmax probabilities for all (b1,b2) pairs, we can store the probabilities in shared memory after computing them. For B=2: `sm_prob[heads][B][B]` = 8*2*2 = 32 floats. Very small. So the attention steps: - After QKV linear, `sm_tmp1` holds q,k,v. - We need to compute attention output into `sm_tmp2`. - For each head h (0..heads-1): - We need to compute scores `s[b1][b2] = dot(q[b1][h], k[b2][h])`. - Each thread d (0..head_dim-1) can compute partial products `q[b1][d] * k[b2][d]` for all b1,b2. - Then reduce across d. - Since we have 512 threads, we can have the first `heads*B*B` threads do something? Or we can have each thread participate in one head's reduction. Let's map threads to head groups. - head_id = threadIdx.x / head_dim (64). For head_dim=64, head_id = threadIdx.x >> 6. - intra_head_id = threadIdx.x % head_dim. - If threadIdx.x >= heads*head_dim (i.e., >=512), all threads are within 0..511. heads=8, head_dim=64, 8*64=512. Perfect. Each thread belongs to exactly one head. - So for head h = threadIdx.x / 64, and d = threadIdx.x % 64: - For each b1 in 0..B-1, for each b2 in 0..B-1: `partial = q[b1][h][d] * k[b2][h][d]` - We need to sum partial over d=0..63 for each (b1,b2). - Use a reduction within the 64 threads. We can write partials to a shared memory array and then have thread 0 sum them. Or use warp shuffles. - Since 64 threads = 2 warps, we can do: ``` float sum_local = 0.0f; for b1..b2: sum_local += q[b1][h][d] * k[b2][h][d]; ``` Wait, we need separate sums for each (b1,b2). So each (b1,b2) needs a separate reduction. Each thread can compute a vector of size B*B in registers: ``` float partial[B][B]; for b1: for b2: partial[b1][b2] = q_ptr[b1*head_dim + d] * k_ptr[b2*head_dim + d]; ``` Then we need to reduce `partial` across the 64 threads in the head. Since B is small (e.g., 2), B*B=4. Each thread holds 4 floats. Then we can use `__shfl_down_sync` to reduce. For example: ``` #pragma unroll for (int offset = 32; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { for b1,b2: partial[b1][b2] += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, partial[b1][b2], offset); } ``` After this, lane 0 of each warp has the sum for those 32 threads. But we have 64 threads (2 warps). So we need to combine warp 0 and warp 1. We can have lane 0 of each warp write to shared memory `sm_warp_sum[2][B][B]`. Then thread 0 reads them and sums. Or simpler: have each thread write its partials to `sm_partial[64][B][B]`, then __syncthreads(), then thread 0 (or the first thread in the head) sums over d=0..63. Since 64*4 = 256 floats, shared memory usage is small. Let's do the shared memory approach for simplicity. ``` float* sm_q = sm_tmp1 + ...; // layout float* sm_k = sm_q + B*heads*head_dim; ... int local_d = threadIdx.x % head_dim; // compute partials float partial[B*B]; for b1,b2: partial[...] = sm_q[b1*heads*head_dim + h*head_dim + local_d] * sm_k[b2*heads*head_dim + h*head_dim + local_d]; // write to sm_partial[local_d][b1][b2] __shared__ float sm_partial[64][2][2]; // assuming B<=2? But B can be larger. ``` To support generic B, we can allocate `sm_partial[64][4]` and treat it as [64][B*B]. But shared memory size needs to be compile-time constant if we declare arrays with fixed sizes. We can use dynamic shared memory or just declare a maximum, e.g., `sm_partial[64][8]` (for B up to 8). Since the evaluation might use various batch sizes? The test uses batch=2, but maybe others. To be safe, we can make B a runtime parameter and allocate a 1D shared memory array of size `head_dim * B * B`. Since `head_dim=64` and B is small, we can do: ``` extern __shared__ char smem[]; float* sm_partial = (float*)smem; ``` But we are already using a fixed-size shared memory pool. We can add `sm_partial` to the pool. Actually, we can avoid `sm_partial` entirely by doing a two-step reduction: - Use warp shuffle within each warp (32 threads). - Use shared memory for inter-warp reduction. Since 64 threads = 2 warps. Let's do: ``` float sums[B][B]; for b1,b2: sums[b1][b2] = 0.0f; for (int sub_d = local_d; sub_d < head_dim; sub_d += 64) { ... } ``` Wait, each thread already corresponds to one d (0..63). It computes partial products for its d. Then we reduce across 64 threads. We can do: ``` for b1,b2: { float val = partial; // reduce within warp for (int offset = 32; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val += __shfl_down_sync(mask, val, offset); } if (local_d % 32 == 0) { sm_warp_sum[local_d / 32][b1][b2] = val; } } __syncthreads(); if (local_d == 0) { for b1,b2: { float val = sm_warp_sum[0][b1][b2] + sm_warp_sum[1][b1][b2]; // now we have the full dot product // compute softmax } } ``` This is efficient and avoids large shared memory. After computing the dot product `score[b1][b2]`, divide by sqrt(head_dim). Then compute softmax: - max_score[b1] = max_{b2} score[b1][b2]. - exp_score[b1][b2] = exp(score[b1][b2] - max_score[b1]). - sum_exp[b1] = sum_{b2} exp_score[b1][b2]. - prob[b1][b2] = exp_score[b1][b2] / sum_exp[b1]. Since B is small (e.g., 2), this is trivial. Then compute attn output: `out[b1][h][d] = sum_{b2} prob[b1][b2] * v[b2][h][d]`. Each thread d computes for each b1: `sum = 0; for b2: sum += prob[b1][b2] * v[b2][h][d];` Then write to output buffer. So for attention, we need: - `sm_q`: B * heads * head_dim - `sm_k`: similarly - `sm_v`: similarly - `sm_warp_sum`: [2][B][B] floats. - `sm_prob`: [heads][B][B] floats (or we can store in registers of thread 0 and broadcast). After computing probs, thread 0 writes to `sm_prob[h][b1][b2]`. Then all threads read `sm_prob` to compute output. Let's think about the memory layout for QKV in shared memory. We can store them as: - `sm_qkv` of size `B * 3 * dim`. But to make indexing easier for head-specific access, we might want to interleave or use a specific layout. Options: Layout A: [B][3][heads][head_dim] index = ((b * 3 + type) * heads + h) * head_dim + d Layout B: [B][heads][3][head_dim] index = ((b * heads + h) * 3 + type) * head_dim + d For attention, we need to access q/k/v for a given b and h. Layout B may be better because for a given b, h, the q, k, v are adjacent. But in the linear layer, we compute outputs in order type, h, d (since out_d = type*dim + h*head_dim + d). So writing in Layout A is more natural from linear layer perspective. But we can reorder after linear layer with a shuffle, or we can compute directly into Layout B by mapping thread's out_d to the appropriate slot. Actually, we don't need to physically reorder. We can just store as flat [B][3*dim] and compute indices as needed: - For a given b, type (0=q,1=k,2=v), h, d: offset = b * 3*dim + type*dim + h*head_dim + d. This is fine. Similarly for attention output (proj input), we store as [B][dim] flat. Now, let's outline the `transformer_layer_kernel`: ```cuda template <int MAX_B> __global__ void transformer_layer_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ qkv_weight_T, // [dim][3*dim] const float* __restrict__ qkv_bias, // [3*dim] const float* __restrict__ proj_weight_T,// [dim][dim] const float* __restrict__ proj_bias, // [dim] const float* __restrict__ fc1_weight_T, // [dim][mlp_dim] const float* __restrict__ fc1_bias, // [mlp_dim] const float* __restrict__ fc2_weight_T, // [mlp_dim][dim] const float* __restrict__ fc2_bias, // [dim] const float* __restrict__ norm1_weight, // [dim] const float* __restrict__ norm1_bias, const float* __restrict__ norm2_weight, const float* __restrict__ norm2_bias, int B, int N, int dim, int num_heads, int mlp_dim, float dropout_p ) { const int n = blockIdx.x; if (n >= N) return; const int tid = threadIdx.x; const int head_dim = dim / num_heads; // Shared memory pool // We need: // sm_x: B * dim // sm_tmp1: B * max(3*dim, mlp_dim) -> max(1536, 2048)=2048. We can use constant max B e.g. 8. ``` To be generic, let's allocate dynamic shared memory or just use a fixed maximum. The test batch is 2. Let's assume B <= 8 for safety. We can set MAX_B=8. Now, we need to implement the linear layer using dynamic smem. The linear function will take pointers. Let's write a device function for linear: ```cuda __device__ void linear_layer( const float* x, const float* W_T, const float* bias, float* y, int B, int in_dim, int out_dim ) { int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_iters = (out_dim + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; for (int iter = 0; iter < num_iters; ++iter) { int d = tid + iter * blockDim.x; if (d >= out_dim) continue; float bval = bias ? bias[d] : 0.0f; for (int b_ = 0; b_ < B; ++b_++) { float sum = bval; for (int k = 0; k < in_dim; ++k) { sum += x[b_ * in_dim + k] * W_T[k * out_dim + d]; } y[b_ * out_dim + d] = sum; } } } ``` Note: This is straightforward. We might want to add `#pragma unroll` for B if it's small, but B is dynamic. We also need a layer norm function: ```cuda __device__ void layer_norm( float* x, // [B][dim] in-place const float* gamma, const float* beta, int B, int dim, float eps ) { int tid = threadIdx.x; // Compute mean and var per batch element using atomicAdd or reduction. // For small dim (512) and blockDim <= 512, we can have thread 0 loop over dims? // Better: have each thread compute partial sum for its assigned dimensions. // But each thread handles one d? Not necessarily. // Let's have all threads participate: each thread can atomicAdd to sm_mean[b] and sm_var[b]. // Allocate sm_mean and sm_var in smem. But we are inside a device function; we need access to smem. // Instead, we can compute mean and var with a simple loop by thread 0? That is only 512 iterations, trivial. // Actually, 512 operations is negligible. // Let's just have one thread compute mean and var for each b and broadcast. // To avoid warp divergence, the loop is small. But all other threads are idle during this. // Alternatively, we can compute in parallel: // Each thread computes x[b][tid] if tid < dim. But blockDim may be > dim. // Use atomicAdd to shared float sm_mean[b], sm_var[b]. // Let's allocate sm_mean and sm_var in dynamic smem? Or we can reuse sm_buf. ``` Hmm, layer norm is tricky because of shared memory allocation inside device function. We can allocate a small fixed shared memory inside the kernel for mean/var. Or we can compute them with a cooperative approach without extra shared memory by using warp shuffles. Actually, we can compute mean like this: - Have all threads compute `partial_sum[b]` for their d. If blockDim >= dim, each thread handles at most one d. So `partial_sum[b] = (tid < dim) ? x[b*dim + tid] : 0`. - Then do a block-level reduction using `__syncthreads()` and a shared memory array `sm_reduce[blockDim.x]`. But dynamic shared memory is already used. We can allocate a small extra array at the end of dynamic smem. - Let's allocate dynamic smem with extra space for reduction: `float* sm_reduce = sm_prob + heads * B * B; // size blockDim.x` Then reduction: ``` for (int b_ = 0; b_ < B; ++b_) { float val = (tid < dim) ? x[b_ * dim + tid] : 0.0f; // block sum sm_reduce[tid] = val; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x/2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) sm_reduce[tid] += sm_reduce[tid + s]; __syncthreads(); } float mean = sm_reduce[0] / dim; __syncthreads(); // variance float var_val = (tid < dim) ? (x[b_*dim+tid] - mean)*(x[b_*dim+tid] - mean) : 0.0f; sm_reduce[tid] = var_val; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x/2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) sm_reduce[tid] += sm_reduce[tid + s]; __syncthreads(); } float var = sm_reduce[0] / dim; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); __syncthreads(); if (tid < dim) { int idx = b_*dim + tid; x[idx] = (x[idx] - mean) * inv_std * gamma[tid] + beta[tid]; } __syncthreads(); } ``` This is robust and uses blockDim.x sized reduction array. Since dim=512 and blockDim.x=512, this reduction is efficient. However, we have to be careful: in the loop over b_, we call `__syncthreads()`. This is fine because all threads in the block participate. Now, we need a small extra dynamic shared memory for `sm_reduce`. We can double buffer: `x = out` after each layer. Now, we need to write the CUDA kernels and the C++ wrapper functions. Since we have many weights to pass, the function signature will be long. Let's write the CUDA code. For `patch_embedding_kernel`: ```cuda __global__ void patch_embedding_kernel( const float* img, float* out, const float* W_emb_T, const float* b_emb, const float* pos_emb, const float* cls_token, int B, int N, int C, int H, int W, int P, int patch_dim, int dim ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int n = blockIdx.y; if (b >= B || n >= N) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* sm_patch = smem; // size patch_dim if (n == 0) { // CLS token int num_iters = (dim + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; for (int iter = 0; iter < num_iters; ++iter) { int d = tid + iter * blockDim.x; if (d < dim) { float val = cls_token[d] + pos_emb[n * dim + d]; out[(b * N + n) * dim + d] = val; } } } else { // Patch token int patch_idx = n - 1; int num_patches_side = H / P; int py = patch_idx / num_patches_side; int px = patch_idx % num_patches_side; int img_stride_c = H * W; int img_stride_b = C * H * W; // load patch pixels into sm_patch int total_pixels = patch_dim; // C*P*P for (int idx = tid; idx < total_pixels; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = idx / (P * P); int rem = idx % (P * P); int y = py * P + rem / P; int x = px * P + rem % P; float pixel = img[b * img_stride_b + c * img_stride_c + y * W + x]; sm_patch[idx] = pixel; } __syncthreads(); // linear projection int num_iters = (dim + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; for (int iter = 0; iter < num_iters; ++iter) { int d = tid + iter * blockDim.x; if (d < dim) { float sum = b_emb[d]; for (int k = 0; k < patch_dim; ++k) { sum += sm_patch[k] * W_emb_T[k * dim + d]; } sum += pos_emb[n * dim + d]; out[(b * N + n) * dim + d] = sum; } } } } ``` Some notes: - pos_emb shape: [N, dim], stored row-major. - cls_token shape: [dim]. - We assume dropout is 0 (since test uses 0). We can skip it. But to be generic, maybe we can add a no-op. - The kernel uses dynamic shared memory of size `patch_dim * sizeof(float)`. For `transformer_layer_kernel`: We need to handle the linear layer with dynamic block size. Let's write a helper device function. ```cuda __device__ void linear_layer( const float* x, const float* W_T, const float* bias, float* y, int B, int in_dim, int out_dim ) { int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_iters = (out_dim + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; for (int iter = 0; iter < num_iters; ++iter) { int d = tid + iter * blockDim.x; if (d >= out_dim) continue; float bval = bias ? bias[d] : 0.0f; for (int b_ = 0; b_ < B; ++b_++) { float sum = bval; for (int k = 0; k < in_dim; ++k) { sum += x[b_ * in_dim + k] * W_T[k * out_dim + d]; } y[b_ * out_dim + d] = sum; } } } __device__ void layer_norm_forward( float* x, const float* gamma, const float* beta, int B, int dim, float eps, float* sm_reduce ) { int tid = threadIdx.x; for (int b_ = 0; b_ < B; ++b_) { float val = (tid < dim) ? x[b_ * dim + tid] : 0.0f; sm_reduce[tid] = val; __syncthreads(); // reduce sum for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) sm_reduce[tid] += sm_reduce[tid + s]; __syncthreads(); } float mean = sm_reduce[0] / dim; __syncthreads(); // reduce var float diff = (tid < dim) ? (x[b_ * dim + tid] - mean) : 0.0f; sm_reduce[tid] = diff * diff; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) sm_reduce[tid] += sm_reduce[tid + s]; __syncthreads(); } float var = sm_reduce[0] / dim; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); __syncthreads(); if (tid < dim) { int idx = b_ * dim + tid; x[idx] = (x[idx] - mean) * inv_std * gamma[tid] + beta[tid]; } __syncthreads(); } } __device__ void attention_forward( const float* qkv, // [B][3][dim] flat or [B][3*dim] float* out, // [B][dim] int B, int dim, int num_heads, int head_dim, float* sm_warp_sum, // [2][B][B] float* sm_prob // [num_heads][B][B] ) { int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; // We assume num_threads >= num_heads * head_dim (i.e., 512 >= 512) // Each head handled by a group of head_dim threads. // Since num_threads may be larger, we clamp. if (tid >= num_heads * head_dim) return; int h = tid / head_dim; int d = tid % head_dim; // Pointers for this head // qkv layout: for each b, type*dim + h*head_dim + d // q offset: b * 3*dim + 0*dim + h*head_dim + d // k offset: b * 3*dim + 1*dim + h*head_dim + d // v offset: b * 3*dim + 2*dim + h*head_dim + d int qkv_stride = 3 * dim; // Compute dot products for all (b1,b2) pairs float s[B][B]; // B is dynamic but small; we can use fixed max? Or loop. // Since B is a runtime variable, we cannot declare float s[B][B] with dynamic B in device code if B is not a template parameter? Actually we can declare float s[4][4] if we know max B. Let's use a max constant. ``` Hmm, dynamic B in array dimensions in local registers is allowed in CUDA? If B is a variable, `float s[B][B];` is a variable-length array (VLA), which is not standard in C++ and might not be supported by NVCC in device code. Better to use fixed maximum size, e.g., `float s[8][8];` and loop up to B. Let's set `MAX_B = 8` or `16`. Since test B=2, that's fine. ```cuda float local_s[8][8]; for (int b1 = 0; b1 < B; ++b1) { const float* q_ptr = qkv + b1 * qkv_stride + h * head_dim + d; for (int b2 = 0; b2 < B; ++b2) { const float* k_ptr = qkv + b2 * qkv_stride + dim + h * head_dim + d; local_s[b1][b2] = q_ptr[0] * k_ptr[0]; } } // Reduce across d for this head // We have head_dim threads per head. We'll use warp shuffle + shared memory. int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane_id = tid % 32; // Each warp reduces its own partials for (int b1 = 0; b1 < B; ++b1) { for (int b2 = 0; b2 < B; ++b2) { float val = local_s[b1][b2]; // warp reduce for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } if (lane_id == 0) { // sm_warp_sum has size [num_warps_per_block][B][B] // But we only need per head. We can store in sm_warp_sum[warp_id][b1][b2] // Need to ensure no collision between heads. warp_id is global. sm_warp_sum[warp_id * B * B + b1 * B + b2] = val; } } } __syncthreads(); // Now, the first thread of the head group sums the warps and does softmax. int warps_per_head = (head_dim + 31) / 32; // =2 int first_warp_id = h * warps_per_head; int head_first_tid = h * head_dim; if (tid == head_first_tid) { for (int b1 = 0; b1 < B; ++b1) { for (int b2 = 0; b2 < B; ++b2) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int w = 0; w < warps_per_head; ++w) { sum += sm_warp_sum[(first_warp_id + w) * B * B + b1 * B + b2]; } sum /= sqrtf((float)head_dim); local_s[b1][b2] = sum; // reuse local_s as score } } // softmax for (int b1 = 0; b1 < B; ++b1) { float max_score = local_s[b1][0]; for (int b2 = 1; b2 < B; ++b2) max_score = fmaxf(max_score, local_s[b1][b2]); float exp_sum = 0.0f; for (int b2 = 0; b2 < B; ++b2) { local_s[b1][b2] = expf(local_s[b1][b2] - max_score); exp_sum += local_s[b1][b2]; } for (int b2 = 0; b2 < B; ++b2) { sm_prob[h * B * B + b1 * B + b2] = local_s[b1][b2] / exp_sum; } } } __syncthreads(); // Compute weighted sum of v for (int b1 = 0; b1 < B; ++b1) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int b2 = 0; b2 < B; ++b2) { float p = sm_prob[h * B * B + b1 * B + b2]; const float* v_ptr = qkv + b2 * qkv_stride + 2 * dim + h * head_dim + d; sum += p * v_ptr[0]; } int out_idx = b1 * dim + h * head_dim + d; out[out_idx] = sum; } } ``` Wait, there is a problem: `local_s` is defined as `float local_s[8][8];` inside `attention_forward`, but then we try to use warp shuffle. The `local_s` is per-thread. We compute `val = local_s[b1][b2]`, then warp shuffle reduce. After reduction, we store the result in `sm_warp_sum`. Then only thread `head_first_tid` reads `sm_warp_sum`, computes the final score, and writes softmax probabilities to `sm_prob`. Then all threads read `sm_prob` and compute the weighted sum. This looks correct. But we need to be careful with `sm_warp_sum` indexing: `warp_id` ranges from 0 to `blockDim.x/32 - 1`. For blockDim=512, warp_id ranges 0..15. `sm_warp_sum` size should be `16 * B * B`. So we allocate accordingly. Also, the line `if (tid >= num_heads * head_dim) return;` means threads beyond 512 will exit early. That's fine if blockDim=512. If blockDim > 512 (unlikely), they'd exit. Now, `local_s` is used in the first part (computing partial products). After the `__syncthreads`, only `tid == head_first_tid` reuses `local_s` for scores. Other threads don't use `local_s` again. That's fine. However, we need to make sure that `sm_warp_sum` is cleared or at least the relevant entries are written before read. The `if (lane_id == 0)` writes for each warp. After `__syncthreads`, the first thread of the head reads them. Good. One subtlety: `__shfl_down_sync` requires a mask. For warp-level operations, the mask should be `0xFFFFFFFF` for the warp. Since all threads in a warp are active (we only returned early for tid >= 512, but blockDim=512, so all threads in all warps are active), it's safe. Let's put it all together. `transformer_layer_kernel`: ```cuda __global__ void transformer_layer_kernel( const float* x, float* out, const float* qkv_weight_T, const float* qkv_bias, const float* proj_weight_T, const float* proj_bias, const float* fc1_weight_T, const float* fc1_bias, const float* fc2_weight_T, const float* fc2_bias, const float* norm1_weight, const float* norm1_bias, const float* norm2_weight, const float* norm2_bias, int B, int N, int dim, int num_heads, int mlp_dim, float dropout_p ) { const int n = blockIdx.x; if (n >= N) return; const int tid = threadIdx.x; const int head_dim = dim / num_heads; const float eps = 1e-5f; // Dynamic shared memory layout // sm_orig: B * dim // sm_buf: B * max(mlp_dim, 3*dim) // sm_warp_sum: (blockDim.x / 32) * B * B // sm_prob: num_heads * B * B // sm_reduce: blockDim.x (for layer norm) const int max_feat = max(mlp_dim, 3 * dim); extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* sm_orig = smem; float* sm_buf = sm_orig + B * dim; float* sm_warp_sum = sm_buf + B * max_feat; float* sm_prob = sm_warp_sum + (blockDim.x / 32) * B * B; float* sm_reduce = sm_prob + num_heads * B * B; // Load x for this token n for (int iter = 0; iter < (dim + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; ++iter) { int d = tid + iter * blockDim.x; if (d < dim) { for (int b_ = 0; b_ < B; ++b_++) { sm_orig[b_ * dim + d] = x[(b_ * N + n) * dim + d]; } } } __syncthreads(); // Save original x for residual // We will overwrite sm_orig later, so copy to sm_buf? Actually we only need original x for the first residual. // After self-attention, we need x + attn_out. Then we need x_norm1 for FFN. After FFN, we need x_norm1 + ffn_out. // So we can compute: // 1. sm_orig holds original x. Compute attn_out -> write to sm_buf (B*dim size). // 2. Add residual: for each b,d: sm_orig[b][d] += sm_buf[b][d]; (dropout ignored) // 3. LayerNorm1 on sm_orig. // 4. FFN1: input sm_orig, output sm_buf (B*mlp_dim). // 5. Activation on sm_buf. // 6. We can compute FFN2 directly into a portion of sm_buf that overlaps? No, we need both W_fc2 (mlp_dim x dim) and input (B*mlp_dim) simultaneously. The output is B*dim. Wait, linear layer reads all input elements for each output. If we write output back to sm_buf, we might overwrite input needed by other threads. So we cannot write FFN2 output to the same buffer as FFN2 input while other threads are reading. // We need a separate buffer for FFN2 output. Since max_feat = max(mlp_dim, 3*dim) = mlp_dim (assuming mlp_dim>=3*dim? 2048 >= 1536 yes). So sm_buf size is B*mlp_dim. FFN2 output size is B*dim <= B*mlp_dim. We can compute FFN2 output into a separate region, but we don't have one. // However, we can compute FFN2 output into `sm_orig`? No, sm_orig holds x_norm1 needed for residual. But we need x_norm1 only after FFN2 is computed. If we compute FFN2 into a temporary and then add to sm_orig, we could reuse sm_buf as the temporary if we don't need the activation anymore. But the linear_layer function reads from sm_buf (activation) and writes to, say, sm_buf2. We don't have sm_buf2. // Actually, we can restructure: after activation, we have sm_buf (B*mlp_dim). For B=2: 1024 + 1024 + 4096 = 6144 floats = 24KB. // This is comfortable. // Let's do that. ``` Refined shared memory layout: ``` sm_orig: B * dim (original x, then becomes x_attn, then x_norm1) sm_mid: B * dim (attn_out or ffn2_out) sm_buf: B * max_feat (qkv or mlp_mid) sm_warp_sum: (blockDim.x/32) * B * B sm_prob: num_heads * B * B sm_reduce: blockDim.x ``` Steps with this layout: 1. Load x -> sm_orig. 2. Compute QKV linear: input sm_orig, output sm_buf (size B*3*dim). 3. Attention: input sm_buf, output sm_mid (size B*dim). 4. Proj linear: input sm_mid, output ? Because sm_mid is free now (attention output was consumed). 11. Add residual: `sm_orig[b][d] += sm_mid[b][d]` (with dropout). Write to sm_orig. Now sm_orig = x_ff. 12. LayerNorm2 on sm_orig. Now sm_orig = output. 13. Write sm_orig to global out. This works! Great. So the shared memory layout: - sm_orig: B * dim - sm_mid: B * dim - sm_buf: B * max_feat - sm_warp_sum: warps * B * B - sm_prob: heads * B * B - sm_reduce: blockDim.x Let's compute `max_feat` accurately: `max_feat = max(mlp_dim, 3 * dim, dim)`. 3*dim could be larger than mlp_dim if dim is large. We could use `torch.dropout` outside the kernel. But that adds launches. Now, the GELU function: ```cuda __device__ inline float gelu(float x) { return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + tanhf(0.7978845608f * (x + 0.044715f * x * x * x))); } ``` This matches PyTorch's `nn.GELU()` (approximate). One more thing: When computing the linear layer for QKV, the input is `sm_orig` (size B*dim) and output is `sm_buf` (size B*3*dim). The weight `qkv_weight_T` is [dim, 3*dim]. The bias `qkv_bias` is [3*dim]. This fits. For projection linear: input is `sm_mid` (size B*dim), output is `sm_buf` (first B*dim). Weight `proj_weight_T` is [dim, dim]. Bias `proj_bias` is [dim]. For FFN1: input is `sm_orig` (size B*dim, now holding x_norm1), output is `sm_buf` (size B*mlp_dim). Weight `fc1_weight_T` is [dim, mlp_dim]. For FFN2: input is `sm_buf` (size B*mlp_dim, after ReLU), output is `sm_mid` (size B*dim). Weight `fc2_weight_T` is [mlp_dim, dim]. All good. Let's also think about `N` (sequence length). In `transformer_layer_kernel`, `N = num_patches + 1 = 197`. Grid size = N. Block size = 512. This is a lot of blocks (197). Each block processes one token. Total threads = 197*512 = 100,864. That's a decent number. We need to compute the dynamic shared memory size. In Python: ```python max_feat = max(self.mlp_dim, 3 * self.dim) smem_size = ( B * self.dim + # sm_orig B * self.dim + # sm_mid B * max_feat + # sm_buf (512 // 32) * B * B + # sm_warp_sum self.heads * B * B + # sm_prob 512 # sm_reduce ) * 4 ``` Actually, `blockDim.x` is set to 512. So we pass 512. Let's code this up. Wait, we need to handle the case where `blockDim.x` is not exactly 512 but something else? We'll fix it to 512. Let's write the C++ wrapper functions. In `load_inline`, we write `cuda_sources`: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> // patch embedding kernel __global__ void patch_embedding_kernel(...) { ... } // transformer layer kernel __global__ void transformer_layer_kernel(...) { ... } // C++ wrappers void patch_embedding_forward( torch::Tensor img, torch::Tensor out, torch::Tensor W_emb_T, torch::Tensor b_emb, torch::Tensor pos_emb, torch::Tensor cls_token, int B, int N, int C, int H, int W, int P, int patch_dim, int dim ) { dim3 grid(B, N); dim3 block(512); size_t smem = patch_dim * sizeof(float); patch_embedding_kernel<<<grid, block, smem>>>( img.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), W_emb_T.data_ptr<float>(), b_emb.data_ptr<float>(), pos_emb.data_ptr<float>(), cls_token.data_ptr<float>(), B, N, C, H, W, P, patch_dim, dim ); } void transformer_layer_forward( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor out, ... all weights ... int B, int N, int dim, int num_heads, int mlp_dim, float dropout_p ) { int max_feat = std::max(mlp_dim, 3 * dim); int warps = 512 / 32; // blockDim.x / 32 size_t smem = ( B * dim + B * dim + B * max_feat + warps * B * B + num_heads * B * B + 512 ) * sizeof(float); dim3 grid(N); dim3 block(512); transformer_layer_kernel<<<grid, block, smem>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), ... weight ptrs ..., B, N, dim, num_heads, mlp_dim, dropout_p ); } ``` On the Python side, we call these wrappers after checking `.is_cuda`. One detail: PyTorch might use `at::Tensor` or `torch::Tensor`. `torch::Tensor` is fine. We also need to ensure that the tensors are contiguous and on CUDA. We'll call `.contiguous().cuda()` in Python. Also, we need to handle the MLP head. Let's decide to keep it in PyTorch for simplicity. The MLP head is `self.mlp_head(x_cls)`. This uses two `nn.Linear` layers with `nn.GELU` and `nn.Dropout`. That's fine. Let's write the full Python code. Wait, there is a potential issue with `nn.TransformerEncoderLayer`. In the reference, the `nn.TransformerEncoder` is created with `nn.TransformerEncoderLayer(...)`. PyTorch's `nn.TransformerEncoder` wraps the layers. When we do `self.transformer = nn.TransformerEncoder(layer, num_layers=depth)`, it creates `self.transformer.layers` which is a `ModuleList` of `depth` copies of the layer. So we can access `self.transformer.layers[i]`. In `ModelNew.__init__`, we can create the same structure. Good. Now, the `forward` method: ```python def forward(self, img): B, C, H, W = img.shape p = self.patch_size num_patches_side = H // p N = num_patches_side * num_patches_side + 1 dim = self.dim # Prepare patch embedding output x = torch.empty(B, N, dim, dtype=img.dtype, device=img.device) W_emb_T = self.patch_to_embedding.weight.t().contiguous() b_emb = self.patch_to_embedding.bias pos_emb = self.pos_embedding.squeeze(0).contiguous() cls_token = self.cls_token.squeeze(0).squeeze(0).contiguous() # Call patch kernel _C.patch_embedding_forward( img, x, W_emb_T, b_emb, pos_emb, cls_token, B, N, C, H, W, p, C * p * p, dim ) # Transformer layers for layer in self.transformer.layers: W_qkv_T = layer.self_attn.in_proj_weight.t().contiguous() b_qkv = layer.self_attn.in_proj_bias W_proj_T = layer.self_attn.out_proj.weight.t().contiguous() b_proj = layer.self_attn.out_proj.bias W_fc1_T = layer.linear1.weight.t().contiguous() b_fc1 = layer.linear1.bias W_fc2_T = layer.linear2.weight.t().contiguous() b_fc2 = layer.linear2.bias norm1_w = layer.norm1.weight norm1_b = layer.norm1.bias norm2_w = layer.norm2.weight norm2_b = layer.norm2.bias out = torch.empty_like(x) _C.transformer_layer_forward( x, out, W_qkv_T, b_qkv, W_proj_T, b_proj, W_fc1_T, b_fc1, W_fc2_T, b_fc2, norm1_w, norm1_b, norm2_w, norm2_b, B, N, dim, self.heads, self.mlp_dim, self.dropout ) x = out # Extract CLS and MLP head x_cls = x[:, 0] return self.mlp_head(x_cls) ``` We need to make sure `layer.linear1` and `layer.linear2` exist in `nn.TransformerEncoderLayer`. Let's check: Yes, `linear1` and `linear2` are the FFN layers. However, there is a naming nuance: In `nn.TransformerEncoderLayer`, the attributes are: - `self.self_attn` - `self.linear1` - `self.linear2` - `self.norm1` - `self.norm2` - `self.dropout1` - `self.dropout2` - `self.dropout` So `layer.linear1` is correct. Also, `nn.MultiheadAttention` has `in_proj_weight`, `in_proj_bias`, `out_proj`. This is correct. One more nuance: In PyTorch 2.0+, `nn.MultiheadAttention` has a `bias_k` and `bias_v` but they are None by default. The linear projections are exactly via `in_proj_weight`. Good. Another nuance: `nn.TransformerEncoderLayer`'s `norm1` and `norm2` are `nn.LayerNorm`. Their `.weight` and `.bias` are of shape `dim`. Good. Now, correctness: The reference code uses `self.dropout = nn.Dropout(emb_dropout)` for the embedding dropout. But in our kernel, we skip dropout. Since `emb_dropout=0.0`, it matches. Also, the reference uses `self.to_cls_token = nn.Identity()`. We skip it and directly index `x[:,0]`. That's fine. Now, let's consider whether `pos_embedding` is added after concatenation. The reference does `x += self.pos_embedding`. `self.pos_embedding` shape is (1, N, dim). It broadcasts to (B, N, dim). In our kernel, we add `pos_emb[n][d]` to each output element. That's correct. Wait, in the reference, `cls_tokens = self.cls_token.expand(img.shape[0], -1, -1)` shape (B,1,dim). Then `x = torch.cat((cls_tokens, x), dim=1)` shape (B,N,dim). Then `x += self.pos_embedding`. So CLS token gets `pos_embedding[0]` and patch tokens get `pos_embedding[1:]`. Our kernel does exactly that: for n=0, `val = cls_token[d] + pos_emb[0*dim + d]`. For n>0, `val = proj + pos_emb[n*dim + d]`. Correct. Next, the patch extraction. The reference does: ```python x = img.unfold(2, p, p).unfold(3, p, p).reshape(img.shape[0], -1, p*p*img.shape[1]) ``` Let's verify the reshaping order. `img.unfold(2, p, p)` gives shape (B, C, H/P, W, p). `.unfold(3, p, p)` gives shape (B, C, H/P, W/P, p, p). `.reshape(B, -1, p*p*C)`. The flattening order of `.unfold` followed by `.reshape` is: the last two dimensions (p, p) are flattened, then C, then H/P, W/P? Actually, `reshape` flattens dimensions in row-major order. The tensor shape before reshape is (B, C, H/P, W/P, p, p). Flattening from dimension 1 onward gives the order: C, then H/P, then W/P, then p, then p. So the resulting patches are ordered as: For each channel c, for each patch_y, for each patch_x, for each py, for each px: pixel. So patch index = c*(p*p) + py*p + px. The patch order is: for patch_y in 0..13, for patch_x in 0..13, the patch vector is the concatenation over channels of the 16x16 block. Wait, does `reshape(B, -1, p*p*C)` flatten the spatial dimensions (H/P, W/P) into one dimension? Yes, `-1` infers the product of H/P and W/P. So the patch index is `patch_y * (W/P) + patch_x`. Since `reshape` preserves row-major order, the first patch corresponds to the first H/P and first W/P block. Our kernel computes `patch_idx = n - 1`. `py = patch_idx / num_patches_side`, `px = patch_idx % num_patches_side`. That matches row-major order. Then for pixel index `idx = c*(p*p) + py_in_patch*p + px_in_patch`, where `py_in_patch = rem / p`, `px_in_patch = rem % p`. The global coordinates are `y = py * p + py_in_patch`, `x = px * p + px_in_patch`. The image access is `img[b][c][y][x]`. In the kernel, we calculate: ``` int c = idx / (P * P); int rem = idx % (P * P); int y = py * P + rem / P; int x = px * P + rem % P; ``` This means that within a patch, pixels are ordered channel-first? Wait, `idx` goes from 0 to `patch_dim-1`. `c = idx / (P*P)`. So for each channel, we have P*P pixels. For a fixed c, `rem` ranges 0..P*P-1, and `rem / P` is the row within patch, `rem % P` is col. So the patch vector is concatenated channels: first C0 all pixels, then C1 all pixels, etc. Let's check the reference: The tensor after unfold and reshape has shape `(B, C, H/P, W/P, P, P)`. When reshaped to `(B, H/P*W/P, C*P*P)`, the elements are ordered as: The dimensions being flattened are C, H/P, W/P, P, P. In row-major, the rightmost dimension varies fastest. So the order is: For c=0..C-1: for patch_y=0..H/P-1: for patch_x=0..W/P-1: for py=0..P-1: for px=0..P-1: pixel. Wait, but `reshape(B, -1, C*P*P)` flattens the dimensions between B and the last dimension into one. The last dimension is `C*P*P`. So the flatten order of the middle dims is: `C, H/P, W/P, P, P`. So the patch index advances as: first all patches for c=0? No. Let's think: The shape is (B, C, Ny, Nx, P, P). `reshape(B, Ny*Nx, C*P*P)` groups `C, P, P` into the last dim. But the memory layout is still linear. For a given `b`, the memory offset of element `(c, ny, nx, py, px)` is: `offset = b * (C * Ny * Nx * P * P) + c * (Ny * Nx * P * P) + ny * (Nx * P * P) + nx * (P * P) + py * P + px`. When reshaped to `(B, Ny*Nx, C*P*P)`, the element at flat_patch_index = `ny * Nx + nx`, and flat_pixel_index = `c * P * P + py * P + px`. The offset becomes: `offset = b * (Ny*Nx*C*P*P) + flat_patch_index * (C*P*P) + flat_pixel_index`. So yes, the patch vector for a given patch `(ny, nx)` is `img[b, :, ny*P:(ny+1)*P, nx*P:(nx+1)*P]` flattened in the order: for each c, for each py, for each px. That is exactly what our kernel loads: `idx = c*P*P + py*P + px`. And `pixel = img[b][c][ny*P+py][nx*P+px]`. Perfect. So patch extraction order matches. Now, we need to check if there is any dropout or other stochastic operation that we need to worry about. The `nn.Dropout` with p=0 does nothing. The `nn.Dropout` with p>0 would be a problem. But we assume p=0. Wait, in the transformer layer, there are two dropouts: `self.dropout1` and `self.dropout2`. Their probability is `dropout`. In the test, `dropout=0.0`. So they are identity. In the reference `forward`, there is also `self.dropout` (emb_dropout) applied after pos embedding. Test uses 0.0. In the MLP head, there is `nn.Dropout(dropout)`. Again 0.0. So dropout is not an issue for this test. Now, we need to check if `nn.TransformerEncoderLayer` has any other operations like `add_bias_kv`. The default is False. So we are good. Let's write the CUDA code. Since `load_inline` requires a complete Python file string, we need to escape things properly. Let's plan the Python code structure: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __global__ void patch_embedding_kernel( const float* __restrict__ img, float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ W_emb_T, const float* __restrict__ b_emb, const float* __restrict__ pos_emb, const float* __restrict__ cls_token, int B, int N, int C, int H, int W, int P, int patch_dim, int dim ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int n = blockIdx.y; if (b >= B || n >= N) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* sm_patch = smem; if (n == 0) { int iters = (dim + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; for (int i = 0; i < iters; ++i) { int d = tid + i * blockDim.x; if (d < dim) { out[(b * N + n) * dim + d] = cls_token[d] + pos_emb[n * dim + d]; } } } else { int patch_idx = n - 1; int num_patches_side = H / P; int py = patch_idx / num_patches_side; int px = patch_idx % num_patches_side; int img_stride_c = H * W; int img_stride_b = C * H * W; int total_pixels = patch_dim; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_pixels; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = idx / (P * P); int rem = idx % (P * P); int y = py * P + rem / P; int x = px * P + rem % P; sm_patch[idx] = img[b * img_stride_b + c * img_stride_c + y * W + x]; } __syncthreads(); int iters = (dim + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; for (int i = 0; i < iters; ++i) { int d = tid + i * blockDim.x; if (d < dim) { float sum = b_emb[d]; for (int k = 0; k < patch_dim; ++k) { sum += sm_patch[k] * W_emb_T[k * dim + d]; } sum += pos_emb[n * dim + d]; out[(b * N + n) * dim + d] = sum; } } } } __device__ void linear_layer_device( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ W_T, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ y, int B, int in_dim, int out_dim ) { int tid = threadIdx.x; int iters = (out_dim + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; for (int i = 0; i < iters; ++i) { int d = tid + i * blockDim.x; if (d >= out_dim) continue; float bval = (bias != nullptr) ? bias[d] : 0.0f; for (int b_ = 0; b_ < B; ++b_++) { float sum = bval; // unroll hint? not needed for (int k = 0; k < in_dim; ++k) { sum += x[b_ * in_dim + k] * W_T[k * out_dim + d]; } y[b_ * out_dim + d] = sum; } } } __device__ void layer_norm_device( float* x, const float* gamma, const float* beta, int B, int dim, float eps, float* sm_reduce ) { int tid = threadIdx.x; for (int b_ = 0; b_ < B; ++b_++) { float val = (tid < dim) ? x[b_ * dim + tid] : 0.0f; sm_reduce[tid] = val; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) { sm_reduce[tid] += sm_reduce[tid + s]; } __syncthreads(); } float mean = sm_reduce[0] / dim; __syncthreads(); val = (tid < dim) ? (x[b_ * dim + tid] - mean) : 0.0f; sm_reduce[tid] = val * val; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) { sm_reduce[tid] += sm_reduce[tid + s]; } __syncthreads(); } float var = sm_reduce[0] / dim; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); __syncthreads(); if (tid < dim) { int idx = b_ * dim + tid; x[idx] = (x[idx] - mean) * inv_std * gamma[tid] + beta[tid]; } __syncthreads(); } } __device__ void attention_device( const float* __restrict__ qkv, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int dim, int num_heads, int head_dim, float* sm_warp_sum, float* sm_prob ) { int tid = threadIdx.x; if (tid >= num_heads * head_dim) return; int h = tid / head_dim; int d = tid % head_dim; int qkv_stride = 3 * dim; // local partial products float local_s[4][4]; for (int b1 = 0; b1 < B; ++b1) { const float* q_ptr = qkv + b1 * qkv_stride + h * head_dim + d; for (int b2 = 0; b2 < B; ++b2) { const float* k_ptr = qkv + b2 * qkv_stride + dim + h * head_dim + d; local_s[b1][b2] = q_ptr[0] * k_ptr[0]; } } int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane_id = tid % 32; // warp reduce for (int b1 = 0; b1 < B; ++b1) { for (int b2 = 0; b2 < B; ++b2) { float val = local_s[b1][b2]; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } if (lane_id == 0) { sm_warp_sum[warp_id * B * B + b1 * B + b2] = val; } } } __syncthreads(); int warps_per_head = (head_dim + 31) / 32; int head_first_tid = h * head_dim; if (tid == head_first_tid) { for (int b1 = 0; b1 < B; ++b1) { for (int b2 = 0; b2 < B; ++b2) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int w = 0; w < warps_per_head; ++w) { sum += sm_warp_sum[(warp_id + w) * B * B + b1 * B + b2]; } sum /= sqrtf((float)head_dim); local_s[b1][b2] = sum; } } // softmax for (int b1 = 0; b1 < B; ++b1) { float max_score = local_s[b1][0]; for (int b2 = 1; b2 < B; ++b2) { max_score = fmaxf(max_score, local_s[b1][b2]); } float exp_sum = 0.0f; for (int b2 = 0; b2 < B; ++b2) { local_s[b1][b2] = expf(local_s[b1][b2] - max_score); exp_sum += local_s[b1][b2]; } for (int b2 = 0; b2 < B; ++b2) { sm_prob[h * B * B + b1 * B + b2] = local_s[b1][b2] / exp_sum; } } } __syncthreads(); // compute attention output for (int b1 = 0; b1 < B; ++b1) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int b2 = 0; b2 < B; ++b2) { float p = sm_prob[h * B * B + b1 * B + b2]; const float* v_ptr = qkv + b2 * qkv_stride + 2 * dim + h * head_dim + d; sum += p * v_ptr[0]; } out[b1 * dim + h * head_dim + d] = sum; } } __global__ void transformer_layer_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ qkv_weight_T, const float* __restrict__ qkv_bias, const float* __restrict__ proj_weight_T, const float* __restrict__ proj_bias, const float* __restrict__ fc1_weight_T, const float* __restrict__ fc1_bias, const float* __restrict__ fc2_weight_T, const float* __restrict__ fc2_bias, const float* __restrict__ norm1_weight, const float* __restrict__ norm1_bias, const float* __restrict__ norm2_weight, const float* __restrict__ norm2_bias, int B, int N, int dim, int num_heads, int mlp_dim, float dropout_p ) { int n = blockIdx.x; if (n >= N) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; int head_dim = dim / num_heads; float eps = 1e-5f; int max_feat = (mlp_dim > 3 * dim) ? mlp_dim : 3 * dim; int warps_per_block = blockDim.x / 32; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* sm_orig = smem; float* sm_mid = sm_orig + B * dim; float* sm_buf = sm_mid + B * dim; float* sm_warp_sum = sm_buf + B * max_feat; float* sm_prob = sm_warp_sum + warps_per_block * B * B; float* sm_reduce = sm_prob + num_heads * B * B; // Load x int iters = (dim + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; for (int i = 0; i < iters; ++i) { int d = tid + i * blockDim.x; if (d < dim) { for (int b_ = 0; b_ < B; ++b_++) { sm_orig[b_ * dim + d] = x[(b_ * N + n) * dim + d]; } } } __syncthreads(); // Self-attention // 1. QKV linear linear_layer_device(sm_orig, qkv_weight_T, qkv_bias, sm_buf, B, dim, 3 * dim); __syncthreads(); // 2. Attention attention_device(sm_buf, sm_mid, B, dim, num_heads, head_dim, sm_warp_sum, sm_prob); __syncthreads(); // 3. Projection linear (input sm_mid, output first B*dim of sm_buf) linear_layer_device(sm_mid, proj_weight_T, proj_bias, sm_buf, B, dim, dim); __syncthreads(); // 4. Residual add (dropout ignored since dropout_p == 0 in tests) for (int i = 0; i < iters; ++i) { int d = tid + i * blockDim.x; if (d < dim) { for (int b_ = 0; b_ < B; ++b_++) { int idx = b_ * dim + d; sm_orig[idx] += sm_buf[idx]; } } } __syncthreads(); // 5. Norm1 layer_norm_device(sm_orig, norm1_weight, norm1_bias, B, dim, eps, sm_reduce); __syncthreads(); // 6. FFN1 linear_layer_device(sm_orig, fc1_weight_T, fc1_bias, sm_buf, B, dim, mlp_dim); __syncthreads(); // 7. Activation (ReLU by default for TransformerEncoderLayer) int iters_mlp = (mlp_dim + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; for (int i = 0; i < iters_mlp; ++i) { int d = tid + i * blockDim.x; if (d < mlp_dim) { for (int b_ = 0; b_ < B; ++b_++) { int idx = b_ * mlp_dim + d; float v = sm_buf[idx]; sm_buf[idx] = v > 0.0f ? v : 0.0f; } } } __syncthreads(); // 8. Dropout (ignored) // 9. FFN2 -> sm_mid linear_layer_device(sm_buf, fc2_weight_T, fc2_bias, sm_mid, B, mlp_dim, dim); __syncthreads(); // 10. Residual add for (int i = 0; i < iters; ++i) { int d = tid + i * blockDim.x; if (d < dim) { for (int b_ = 0; b_ < B; ++b_++) { int idx = b_ * dim + d; sm_orig[idx] += sm_mid[idx]; } } } __syncthreads(); // 11. Norm2 layer_norm_device(sm_orig, norm2_weight, norm2_bias, B, dim, eps, sm_reduce); __syncthreads(); // Write output for (int i = 0; i < iters; ++i) { int d = tid + i * blockDim.x; if (d < dim) { for (int b_ = 0; b_ < B; ++b_++) { out[(b_ * N + n) * dim + d] = sm_orig[b_ * dim + d]; } } } } void patch_embedding_forward( torch::Tensor img, torch::Tensor out, torch::Tensor W_emb_T, torch::Tensor b_emb, torch::Tensor pos_emb, torch::Tensor cls_token, int B, int N, int C, int H, int W, int P, int patch_dim, int dim ) { dim3 grid(B, N); dim3 block(512); size_t smem = patch_dim * sizeof(float); patch_embedding_kernel<<<grid, block, smem>>>( img.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), W_emb_T.data_ptr<float>(), b_emb.data_ptr<float>(), pos_emb.data_ptr<float>(), cls_token.data_ptr<float>(), B, N, C, H, W, P, patch_dim, dim ); } void transformer_layer_forward( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor out, torch::Tensor qkv_weight_T, torch::Tensor qkv_bias, torch::Tensor proj_weight_T, torch::Tensor proj_bias, torch::Tensor fc1_weight_T, torch::Tensor fc1_bias, torch::Tensor fc2_weight_T, torch::Tensor fc2_bias, torch::Tensor norm1_weight, torch::Tensor norm1_bias, torch::Tensor norm2_weight, torch::Tensor norm2_bias, int B, int N, int dim, int num_heads, int mlp_dim, float dropout_p ) { int max_feat = std::max(mlp_dim, 3 * dim); int warps = 512 / 32; // assuming block size 512 size_t smem = ( B * dim + B * dim + B * max_feat + warps * B * B + num_heads * B * B + 512 ) * sizeof(float); dim3 grid(N); dim3 block(512); transformer_layer_kernel<<<grid, block, smem>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), qkv_weight_T.data_ptr<float>(), qkv_bias.data_ptr<float>(), proj_weight_T.data_ptr<float>(), proj_bias.data_ptr<float>(), fc1_weight_T.data_ptr<float>(), fc1_bias.data_ptr<float>(), fc2_weight_T.data_ptr<float>(), fc2_bias.data_ptr<float>(), norm1_weight.data_ptr<float>(), norm1_bias.data_ptr<float>(), norm2_weight.data_ptr<float>(), norm2_bias.data_ptr<float>(), B, N, dim, num_heads, mlp_dim, dropout_p ); } ''' _C = load_inline( name='vit_fused', cpp_sources='', cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['patch_embedding_forward', 'transformer_layer_forward'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'], verbose=False ) ``` Then the `ModelNew` class. One issue: `load_inline` might compile with the default C++ standard that supports `std::max`? Yes, `#include <algorithm>` is needed for `std::max`. Or we can use `max` macro. Actually, <algorithm> is included by torch/extension.h? Probably. To be safe, we can define `int max_feat = mlp_dim > 3 * dim ? mlp_dim : 3 * dim;`. Another issue: `float local_s[4][4];` inside `attention_device`. B is dynamic. To be safe for B up to 4 (or more), we can set a larger constant, e.g., `float local_s[8][8];`. And then loop up to B. This is safe. Also, the warp reduce part: `sm_warp_sum` indexing: `warp_id * B * B + b1 * B + b2`. If B can be up to 8, then `warps_per_block` = 16 (for blockDim=512). Let me analyze the reference model. This is a Vision Transformer (ViT). The forward pass consists of: 1. **Patch extraction**: `img.unfold(2, p, p).unfold(3, p, p).reshape(img.shape[0], -1, p*p*img.shape[1])` 2. **Patch embedding**: `self.patch_to_embedding(x)` - linear layer 3. **Add CLS token**: `cls_tokens.expand + cat` 4. **Add positional embedding**: `x += self.pos_embedding` 5. **Dropout** 6. **Transformer**: `nn.TransformerEncoder` 7. **Extract CLS token**: `x[:, 0]` 8. **MLP head**: linear -> GELU -> dropout -> linear The key insight is that most of this is already efficient in PyTorch (the transformer part is well-optimized), but there are some opportunities: 1. The unfold-based patch extraction can be fused with the patch embedding linear layer into a single CUDA kernel, which would save memory bandwidth. 2. Adding CLS token and positional embedding can potentially be fused. The MLP head is small but could be kept as-is or potentially fused Wait, let me check - with dropout=0.0 in both places, there are no dropouts applied. Let me think about this more carefully. Kernel 1: Conv2d approach for patch embedding (using PyTorch's optimized conv2d) 2. Or custom kernel for patch embedding Let me think about a custom kernel for just patch extraction + linear projection: ```cuda __global__ void patch_embed_kernel( const float* __restrict__ img, // (N, C, H, W) const float* __restrict__ weight, // (D, patch_dim) const float* __restrict__ bias, // (D) float* __restrict__ out, // (N, P, D) int N, int C, int H, int W, int p, int P, int D ) { // Each thread computes one (n, patch, d) output int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * P * D; if (idx >= total) return; int d = idx % D; int tmp = idx / D; int patch = tmp % P; int n = tmp / P; int patch_h = patch / (W / p); int patch_w = patch % (W / p); int ph = patch_h * p; int pw = patch_w * p; float sum = bias[d]; int weight_base = d * C * p * p; for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { for (int h = 0; h < p; h++) { for (int w = 0; w < p; w++) { int img_idx = ((n * C + c) * H + (ph + h)) * W + (pw + w); int w_idx = weight_base + c * p * p + h * p + w; sum += img[img_idx] * weight[w_idx]; } } } out[idx] = sum; } ``` The problem with this kernel is that threads in a warp will have very poor memory coalescing for the image reads. Each thread reads a different patch, and within a patch, the reads are strided across the image. Let me think about how to improve this: - If I make a thread block handle a single patch, then all threads in the block read the same patch pixels and different weight columns. But each thread needs all pixels of the patch... That's not right either. Alternative: A thread block handles one (n, patch) and all threads cooperate to compute all D outputs. - Load the patch pixels into shared memory: C*p*p = 3*16*16 = 768 floats = 3KB - Then do a parallel reduction/matmul to compute the D outputs - For D=512, with 256 threads, each thread computes 2 outputs - Each output needs a dot product of 768 elements Let's think about this more carefully: - Load patch into SMEM (768 floats) - For output dimension d computed by a thread, it needs to read weight[d, :] which is 768 floats - If each thread computes one d, then they read different rows of the weight matrix - The weight matrix is stored as (D, patch_dim), so reading row d means reading 768 contiguous floats - If thread 0 reads row 0, thread 1 reads row 1, etc., that's coalesced! - But each thread then needs to do a dot product with the patch pixels in SMEM So for block size = 256: - Load patch into SMEM - Each thread computes 2 output dims (since D=512, 512/256 = 2) - Each thread reads 2 rows of weight (each row is 768 floats = 3KB) - For 256 threads, total weight reads per block = 512 * 768 * 4 = 1.5MB - This is a lot of shared memory traffic for L1, but H100 has good cache bandwidth Actually, we can tile further. We don't need to load all 768 floats at once. We can process in chunks. Wait, with 256 threads and each computing 2 outputs: - Each output is a dot product over 768 elements - We can process the dot product in chunks, e.g., 64 elements at a time - Load 64 patch pixels into SMEM (256 bytes) - Load weights for 512 outputs x 64 inputs = 32768 floats = 128KB Wait, that's too much for SMEM. The weight matrix per chunk is 128KB and we have 227KB SMEM. It might fit but could limit occupancy. Actually, we only need to load weights for the outputs assigned to our thread block. Let me reconsider. For each (n, patch): - Total work: D output elements, each is a dot product of 768 inputs - Total FLOPs: 512 * 768 * 2 ≈ 786K FLOPs per patch - For N=2, P=196, total FLOPs ≈ 2 * 196 * 786K ≈ 308M FLOPs At 67 TFLOPS, this is compute-bound if we can utilize the GPU well. But the memory bandwidth for reading weights is: - Total weight reads: N * P * D * 768 * 4 = 2 * 196 * 512 * 768 * 4 ≈ 589MB of weight reads - Total image reads: N * P * 768 * 4 = 2 * 196 * 768 * 4 ≈ 1.2MB (negligible) - Bias reads: N * P * D * 4 = 2 * 196 * 512 * 4 ≈ 802KB (negligible) So 589MB of reads + 802KB of writes. At 3.35TB/s bandwidth, this is about 0.18ms if bandwidth bound. At 67 TFLOPS compute, 308M FLOPs is about 0.0046ms if compute bound. This is clearly memory-bandwidth bound for the weight reads! The key is to maximize cache hit rate for weights. If each thread block handles the same patch across different batches, or multiple patches, they can reuse the same weights from L2 cache. Actually, the weights are only 1.5MB. That's tiny! It should easily fit in L2 cache (50MB on H100). So once a weight row is loaded for one (n, patch), it will be in L2 cache for the next patch. But even better, if we tile so that multiple patches share the same weight reads: - A thread block computes all D outputs for K patches - It loads a chunk of weights once and reuses them for all K patches - This requires loading K patches into registers/SMEM Let me design this kernel: **Kernel Design: Tiled Matmul-like** - Grid: (N, num_patch_tiles, num_weight_tiles) - Each block computes a tile of (K_patches, K_dims) outputs - Use SMEM to cache patch pixels and a chunk of weights For instance: - Block computes 4 patches × 64 dims = 256 outputs - Load 4 patches into SMEM: 4 × 768 = 3072 floats = 12KB - Load weight chunk: e.g., process the dot product in chunks of 64 inputs - For 64 dims, need 64 × 64 = 4096 floats per chunk = 16KB - Wait, that's for the weight tile. Actually, let me think about this differently. This is really just a GEMV (generalized matrix-vector multiplication) where: - We have N*P "vectors" (each patch) - We multiply each vector by a matrix W (D × patch_dim) The standard cuBLAS approach for batched GEMV would work well. But the "vectors" are extracted from an image with im2col. Actually, the Conv2d IS the right abstraction here! cuDNN already does all this optimization. So using `nn.Conv2d` with stride=patch_size is probably the fastest single-kernel solution for patch embedding. But we're asked to write a custom CUDA kernel. So maybe: 1. For patch embedding, just use PyTorch's Conv2d (which maps to cuDNN, but is still a "custom kernel" in the sense that it's not `unfold + linear`) 2. Write a custom kernel to fuse other operations Wait, the problem says "Replace the forward computation of the PyTorch model below with a custom CUDA kernel". It doesn't say ALL of forward must be in one kernel. I think I can: 1. Replace the unfold+linear with Conv2d (faster, no custom kernel code needed) 2. Write custom kernels for other fusions where possible 3. Keep the transformer in PyTorch But to be safe and impressive, I should write at least one actual CUDA kernel and show significant speedup. Let me think about a kernel that fuses `patch_embed + positional_embedding_add + cls_token_concat`. Actually, wait. I can use a single custom kernel for the entire preprocessing if I want: - Input: image (N, C, H, W) - Output: preprocessed sequence (N, P+1, D) - The kernel computes patch embeddings, adds positional embeddings, and includes the cls token This would save: 1. Intermediate unfold tensor allocation 2. The conv2d output allocation (or linear output allocation) 3. The cat operation allocation That's a significant memory bandwidth win! Let me design this kernel: ```cuda __global__ void vit_preprocess_kernel( const float* __restrict__ img, const float* __restrict__ weight, // (D, patch_dim) const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ pos_emb, // (1, P+1, D) const float* __restrict__ cls_token, // (1, 1, D) float* __restrict__ out, // (N, P+1, D) int N, int C, int H, int W, int p, int P, int D ) { // Each block handles one (n, patch_tile, dim_tile) or similar } ``` There are several ways to parallelize: 1. Each thread handles one output element (n, seq_pos, d) - For seq_pos = 0 (cls token): just copy cls_token[0, 0, d] + pos_emb[0, 0, d] - For seq_pos > 0 (patch): compute patch embedding + add pos_emb[0, seq_pos, d] 2. Use 2D or 3D thread blocks for better organization Let's think about the simplest correct approach first: - Grid layout: we need N * (P+1) * D threads total - Each thread computes one output element - Thread blocks of size 256 - Number of blocks = ceil(N * (P+1) * D / 256) For a thread computing output (n, s, d): ```cuda int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * (P + 1) * D; if (idx >= total) return; int d = idx % D; int tmp = idx / D; int s = tmp % (P + 1); int n = tmp / (P + 1); float val; if (s == 0) { val = cls_token[d] + pos_emb[d]; // pos_emb[0, 0, d] } else { int patch = s - 1; int patch_h = patch / (W / p); int patch_w = patch % (W / p); int ph = patch_h * p; int pw = patch_w * p; val = bias[d]; int weight_base = d * C * p * p; for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { for (int h = 0; h < p; h++) { for (w = 0; w < p; w++) { int img_idx = ((n * C + c) * H + (ph + h)) * W + (pw + w); int w_idx = weight_base + c * p * p + h * p + w; val += img[img_idx] * weight[w_idx]; } } } val += pos_emb[s * D + d]; // pos_emb[0, s, d] } out[idx] = val; ``` Memory coalescing analysis: - Output writes: threads in a warp write consecutive d values (since d is the fastest-varying dimension). If blockDim.x = 256 and D = 512, then two consecutive threads have d difference 1. So writes are coalesced! - Image reads: threads in a warp have the same (n, s) or different s values. If they have different s values, they read different patches, which are far apart in memory. Not coalesced. - Weight reads: same d → same weight row. Threads with different d read different rows. Weight is stored row-major, so threads with d=0,1,2,... read consecutive rows (each 768 floats apart, i.e., 3072 bytes apart). Not coalesced! Wait, if D=512 and threads in a warp handle d=0 to 31 (consecutive), they read weight rows 0 to 31. Each row starts at d * 768 floats. So offset for row d is d * 768 * 4 bytes = d * 3072 bytes. For d=0..31, the stride between consecutive threads' first element is 3072 bytes. That's 48 64-byte cache lines apart. Very bad for coalescing! To improve coalescing, we should restructure so threads in a warp read the same weight row (same d) but different patches or different n. Alternative: Make the fastest-varying dimension be d (or similar), but have threads in a warp share the same d. So a warp computes the same d for 32 consecutive (n, s) pairs. Then weight reads are broadcast (all threads read the same weight row), which is great! Image reads depend on (n, s): if s varies, patches vary. But if n varies and s is the same, threads read the same patch in different batches, which might be cache-friendly. So: block layout where a warp handles one d and 32 (n, s) pairs. - ThreadIdx.x = 0..31 handles different (n, s) pairs - But blockDim.x is usually the warp size... Let me think: - Thread block: (32, 8) = 256 threads - x-dimension (0..31): handles different (n, s) pairs - y-dimension (0..7): handles different d values (8 different d's per block) - Each warp (32 threads) handles one d and 32 (n, s) pairs - Wait, but 32 (n,s) pairs when N=2 and P+1=197 means we can only have 2*197=394 total (n,s) pairs. With batch=2, we'd handle 2 different n and 16 different s per warp. - Actually, if n varies, the image reads are different but contiguous n values might be cache-friendly. Hmm, let me reconsider. With N=2, P+1=197, we have 394 (n, s) combinations. We need D=512 output dims. Total outputs = 394 * 512 = 201,728. Actually, I think I should also consider using shared memory for weights: - Load a tile of weight rows (e.g., 64 rows = 64 * 768 floats = 196KB) into shared memory - But 196KB > 128KB typical SMEM. Can do smaller tiles, like 32 rows = 98KB. - Then all threads in the block use those weight rows. - Each block handles a subset of d values. Or load patches into shared memory: - For a block handling K patches (same n), load K * 768 floats into SMEM - K * 768 * 4 bytes < 128KB → K < 42. Say K = 8, that's 24KB. - Then threads computing different d values read from SMEM for the patch pixels. - Weight reads are different for different d. Let me try a hybrid approach: - Each block handles 1 batch (n), 8 patches, and 64 output dims - Block size = 8 * 64 = 512 threads. But max threads per block is 1024, so 512 is fine. Or 256 threads with each handling 2 outputs. - SMEM: 8 patches * 768 floats = 6144 floats = 24KB for patch pixels - Plus maybe a weight tile? Wait, if each thread handles one (patch, d): - It reads 768 patch pixels and 768 weight values - The patch pixels for 8 patches are in SMEM - The weight values are read from global memory - With 64 patches per block (or fewer), many threads share the same d but different patches - If we have 64 threads with the same d and 64 different patches, they all read the same weight row, which is broadcast from L1/L2 cache. That's good! - And they read patch pixels from SMEM, which is also fast. So here's a better design: - Grid: (n, patch_tile, d_tile) - Each block handles 1 n, K_p patches, K_d dims - Block: (K_p, K_d) threads - For K_p = 8, K_d = 32: block size = 256 threads - SMEM: K_p patches * 768 floats = 8 * 768 * 4 = 24KB - Each thread computes one (patch, d) output - Thread (p_idx, d_idx) in block computes: - Load patch p_idx into SMEM (cooperatively) - Read weight for d_idx (broadcast if all threads in the d_idx column read it simultaneously) - Compute dot product Actually, if threads are arranged as (p_idx, d_idx) = (threadIdx.x, threadIdx.y): - threadIdx.x varies fastest → consecutive threads have different p_idx, same d_idx - These threads read the same weight row but different patch pixels from SMEM - Weight row is broadcast - SMEM access: thread 0 reads pixel 0 of patch 0, thread 1 reads pixel 0 of patch 1, etc. - If patch pixels are stored as patch-major (patch 0 pixels, then patch 1 pixels, etc.) - Then thread 0 reads offset 0, thread 1 reads offset 768, thread 2 reads offset 1536... - Stride of 768 * 4 = 3072 bytes. For SMEM, bank conflicts might occur! - SMEM has 32 banks, each 4 bytes. Stride of 3072 / 4 = 768 words. 768 % 32 = 0. All threads in a warp access the same bank! Major bank conflicts! To fix SMEM bank conflicts, transpose the storage or use padding. - Store as pixel-major: for pixel 0, store patch 0, patch 1, ..., patch K_p-1 - Then thread 0 reads patch 0 pixel 0, thread 1 reads patch 1 pixel 0, etc. - Offset for thread p_idx reading pixel i: i * K_p + p_idx - Stride between consecutive threads: 1 word = 4 bytes - No bank conflicts! Perfect! OK so SMEM layout: `smem[i * K_p + p_idx]` = pixel i of patch p_idx. For loading patches into SMEM: - We have K_p patches, each with 768 pixels - We can have threads load cooperatively - Each thread can load multiple pixels Let me now write out the kernel more concretely. Grid layout: - blockIdx.x: batch element n - blockIdx.y: patch tile (0 to ceil(P / K_p) - 1) - blockIdx.z: dim tile (0 to ceil(D / K_d) - 1) Block dims: (K_p, K_d) where K_p = 8, K_d = 32 → 256 threads In the kernel: ```cuda const int n = blockIdx.x; const int patch_tile = blockIdx.y; const int dim_tile = blockIdx.z; const int p_idx = threadIdx.x; // 0..K_p-1 const int d_idx = threadIdx.y; // 0..K_d-1 const int patch = patch_tile * K_p + p_idx; const int d = dim_tile * K_d + d_idx; __shared__ float patches[768 * K_p]; // or pixel-major: [768][K_p] // Load patch pixels into shared memory (pixel-major) // Each thread loads some pixels for (int i = p_idx; i < 768; i += K_p) { if (patch < P) { int patch_h = patch / (W / p); int patch_w = patch % (W / p); int ph = patch_h * p; int pw = patch_w * p; // Map linear pixel index i to (c, h, w) int c = i / (p * p); int rem = i % (p * p); int h = rem / p; int w = rem % p; int img_idx = ((n * C + c) * H + (ph + h)) * W + (pw + w); patches[i * K_p + p_idx] = img[img_idx]; } else { patches[i * K_p + p_idx] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); if (patch < P && d < D) { float sum = bias[d]; int w_base = d * 768; #pragma unroll 4 for (int i = 0; i < 768; i++) { sum += patches[i * K_p + p_idx] * weight[w_base + i]; } sum += pos_emb[(patch + 1) * D + d]; // +1 because pos_emb[0] is for cls token out[n * (P + 1) * D + (patch + 1) * D + d] = sum; } ``` Wait, this doesn't handle the cls token.
