But actually, I realize that for 200K outputs, with each needing 768 iterations, that's ~154M iterations total. At 256 threads per block, we need ~600 blocks. On H100 with many SMs, this is fine. Wait, each thread does 768 iterations over the patch pixels. With 200K threads, that's 153M iterations total. Each iteration does 2 FLOPs (multiply-add) and 2 memory reads (one from image, one from weight). If both are L2 hits, this is compute bound. 308M FLOPs at 67 TFLOPS = 4.6 microseconds. That's incredibly fast. But in practice, there will be overhead. Still, the kernel should be very fast. However, the image data layout is NCHW. Each thread reads a different patch. Patch pixels are not contiguous for a single patch in NCHW format (they're strided across H and W). Let me trace: For patch at (ph, pw), the pixels are: img[n, c, ph + h, pw + w] = base + c * H * W + (ph + h) * W + (pw + w) = base + c * H * W + ph * W + pw + h * W + w For a fixed (n, patch, c), as h and w vary: - h=0, w=0: offset = c*H*W + ph*W + pw - h=0, w=1: offset = c*H*W + ph*W + pw + 1 - ... - h=0, w=15: offset = c*H*W + ph*W + pw + 15 - h=1, w=0: offset = c*H*W + ph*W + pw + W So within a single (c, h), the w values are contiguous (stride 1). Across h, stride is W (224). Across c, stride is H*W (50176). For a single thread reading all pixels of one patch, it reads in pattern: for c in 0..2: for h in 0..15: for w in 0..15: read offset + h*W + w (for fixed c and base) This is a series of 16-byte contiguous reads (4 floats), then a jump of (W-16) floats, repeated 16 times, then a jump of (H*W - 16*W) floats. This pattern is actually very cache-friendly! Each 16-float line is read contiguously, which is 64 bytes = one cache line. Then there's a jump to the next line in the same column. The L1 cache on H100 can handle this. But the issue is coalescing across a warp. In a 1D kernel where each thread handles a different (n, patch, d): - Thread 0 (n=0, patch=0, d=0): reads patch 0 pixels - Thread 1 (n=0, patch=0, d=1): reads patch 0 pixels (same as thread 0!) - Thread 2 (n=0, patch=0, d=2): reads patch 0 pixels - ... - Thread 511 (n=0, patch=0, d=511): reads patch 0 pixels All 512 threads in a block have the same n and patch! They all read the SAME patch pixels, but DIFFERENT weight rows. Oh! This is completely different from what I thought. If consecutive threads have consecutive d values, then for a block of 512 threads: - All threads read the SAME patch pixels (but from different n and/or patch) - Wait, let me recalculate. Total outputs = N * P * D = 2 * 196 * 512 = 200,704. If blockDim.x = 512: - Block 0: threads 0..511 - thread 0: d=0, patch=0, n=0 - thread 1: d=1, patch=0, n=0 - ... - thread 511: d=511, patch=0, n=0 - Block 1: threads 512..1023 - thread 512: d=0, patch=1, n=0 - etc. So within a warp (32 threads): - Threads 0..31: d=0..31, patch=0, n=0 - They all read the SAME patch pixels (patch 0, n=0)! - But they read DIFFERENT weight rows (d=0..31) This means: - Image reads: all threads in a warp read the same addresses. The hardware broadcasts this! Only 1 cache line is loaded, and all 32 threads get it. This is actually OPTIMAL! - Weight reads: threads read consecutive rows. Row d is at offset d * 768 floats = d * 3072 bytes. Stride between threads = 3072 bytes. Not coalesced, but each thread only reads its own row. Wait, but threads in a warp don't read the same weight address. They read different rows. The memory controller will try to coalesce these. With stride 3072 bytes: - Thread 0 reads row 0: bytes 0..3071 - Thread 1 reads row 1: bytes 3072..6143 - Thread 2 reads row 2: bytes 6144..9215 - ... - Thread 31 reads row 31: bytes 98304..101375 These are all in different 128-byte cache lines (well, row 0 spans 24 cache lines, row 1 spans the next 24, etc.). So the warp issues 32 * 24 = 768 memory requests. That's terrible! Actually, let me reconsider. Each thread reads 768 floats one by one in a loop: ```cuda for (int i = 0; i < 768; i++) { float pixel = patch_pixels[i]; float w = weight[d * 768 + i]; sum += pixel * w; } ``` At iteration i: - All threads in the warp read patch_pixels[i]. This is a broadcast, great! - Thread d reads weight[d * 768 + i]. The addresses are d * 768 + i. For d=0..31 and fixed i: - Addresses: i, i+768, i+1536, ..., i+24576 - In bytes (multiplying by 4): 4i, 4i+3072, 4i+6144, ..., 4i+98304 - The difference between consecutive threads is 3072 bytes = 48 cache lines (at 64 bytes each) or 24 segments (at 128 bytes each) This is definitely not coalesced. Each thread issues its own memory request for the weight. But wait! On H100, L2 cache bandwidth is 3.35 TB/s. Reading 768 floats * 4 bytes = 3KB per thread. For 32 threads = 96KB per warp. At 3.35TB/s, reading 96KB takes 0.03 microseconds. And this is per iteration over all 24 warps in a block... Actually per warp per block. A warp does 768 iterations. In each iteration, it reads 96KB of weights total. Over 768 iterations, it reads 96KB * 768 = 73.7MB of weights. But it's the SAME 73.7MB for every warp that processes patch 0! Oh right! Different warps in the same block process different d values but the SAME patch. Wait no: - Warp 0: d=0..31, patch=0 - Warp 1: d=32..63, patch=0 - Warp 2: d=64..95, patch=0 - Warp 3: d=96..127, patch=0 - ... - For D=512, there are 16 warps per block, all processing patch=0. So block 0 processes d=0..511 for patch=0. It reads all 512 rows of weights. Total weight read per block = 512 * 768 * 4 = 1.5MB. Block 1 processes patch=1, n=0. It reads the SAME 1.5MB of weights again! But these reads will hit L2 cache because block 0 just loaded them (assuming the cache is large enough, which it is: 50MB >> 1.5MB). So the weight reads are L2 cache hits, which are very fast. So the actual memory bottleneck is: - Image reads: broadcast within a warp, very fast, mostly L2 hits - Weight reads: initially L2 misses for the first block, then L2 hits for subsequent blocks. Total weight bandwidth = N * P * 1.5MB = 2 * 196 * 1.5MB = 588MB of weight traffic (but realistically less because of L2 hits). - Output writes: ~800KB At 3.35 TB/s, 588MB takes ~0.18ms. But with L2 caching, subsequent reads are much faster (L2 bandwidth is typically 10-20x DRAM bandwidth). So total time might be dominated by compute: 308M FLOPs at maybe 10-20 TFLOPS effective = 15-30 microseconds. This is all very approximate, but it suggests the naive kernel might actually work quite well due to H100's large L2 cache! Let me proceed with a slightly optimized kernel and see. Actually, I had another idea: since all threads in a warp read the same patch pixels, we should load the patch pixels into registers or shared memory once per warp. But because the patch is the same for all threads in a block that share the same patch, we could load it into shared memory once per block. For a block processing one patch with 512 threads (16 warps): - Load 768 patch pixels into shared memory (~3KB) - Each thread then reads from shared memory instead of global memory - This saves global memory bandwidth for image reads - But the image reads were already broadcast, so it might not save much Actually, with 16 warps reading the same patch pixels, the hardware cache handles this. But explicit SMEM is more predictable. Let me write a kernel using shared memory for the patch: ```cuda __global__ void vit_embed_kernel( const float* __restrict__ img, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ pos_emb, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C, int H, int W, int P, int D, int p ) { int tid = threadIdx.x; int n = blockIdx.x; int patch = blockIdx.y; int d = tid; // each thread handles one d if (d >= D) return; __shared__ float patch_pixels[768]; // Load patch pixels cooperatively for (int i = tid; i < 768; i += blockDim.x) { int c = i / (p * p); int rem = i % (p * p); int h = rem / p; int w = rem % p; int patch_h = patch / (W / p); int patch_w = patch % (W / p); int img_idx = ((n * C + c) * H + (patch_h * p + h)) * W + (patch_w * p + w); patch_pixels[i] = img[img_idx]; } __syncthreads(); float sum = bias[d]; int w_base = d * 768; #pragma unroll 16 for (int i = 0; i < 768; i++) { sum += patch_pixels[i] * weight[w_base + i]; } sum += pos_emb[(patch + 1) * D + d]; out[n * (P + 1) * D + (patch + 1) * D + d] = sum; } ``` Grid: (N, P) blocks Block: D threads (512) Total threads launched: N * P * D = 200,704 But block size 512 means each block handles 1 patch and all 512 dims. Number of blocks = N * P = 392. On H100 with ~100+ SMs, this is plenty for parallelism. Wait, 392 blocks with 512 threads each. Each block needs to load 768 floats into SMEM. The image reads within a block are coalesced? Let's check: - Thread 0 reads pixel 0, thread 1 reads pixel 1, ..., thread 511... but we only have 768 pixels and 512 threads. - For i = tid, threads 0..511 read pixels 0..511. These are consecutive! Coalesced! - For i = tid + 512, threads 0..255 read pixels 512..767. Also coalesced. Great! The patch loading is coalesced. Then each thread computes one output d. It reads weight row d from global memory. Threads 0..511 read rows 0..511. Not coalesced (stride 3072 bytes). But as discussed, this will be L2 cached. Wait, there are only 392 blocks. The first 32 blocks (one per SM) will load the weights into L2. Then the remaining blocks will have L2 hits. But 392 blocks is not that many. Still, on H100, 392 blocks should be fine. Actually, I realize there's another optimization: instead of each thread reading its entire weight row (768 floats) from global memory, we can tile the computation. But with the weights in L2 cache and the loop being very predictable, the compiler might generate good code. One more thing: `#pragma unroll 16` on a loop of 768 iterations means 48 unrolled iterations. That's a lot of code. Maybe `#pragma unroll 8` or no unroll is better. I'll let the compiler decide with just `#pragma unroll`. Actually, for weight reads, I could make the memory access pattern more cache-friendly by having threads in a warp process the same d. But with block size = D = 512, a warp already processes 32 consecutive d values. Alternatively, I could transpose the weight matrix to (768, 512) and have threads in a warp read consecutive elements within the same column... but that changes which weight each thread needs. If I store weights as (768, 512) instead of (512, 768): - Thread d needs column d - At iteration i, thread d reads weight[i * 512 + d] - Threads in a warp (d=0..31) read: i*512, i*512+1, ..., i*512+31 - These are consecutive! Fully coalesced! YES! This is the key insight. If I transpose the weight matrix, weight reads become fully coalesced! So: - Weight shape in host: (D, patch_dim) = (512, 768) - In kernel, pass as transposed: (patch_dim, D) = (768, 512) - Thread d reads column d - At iteration i: weight[i * D + d] This is beautiful! The 768 iterations now read coalesced memory! Wait, I need to verify. nn.Linear weight is stored as (out_features, in_features). So `self.patch_to_embedding.weight` has shape (512, 768). I can pass `self.patch_to_embedding.weight.t().contiguous()` to the kernel, or create a transposed copy during initialization. Let me update the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void vit_embed_kernel( const float* __restrict__ img, const float* __restrict__ weight_t, // transposed: (768, 512) const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ pos_emb, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C, int H, int W, int P, int D, int p ) { int tid = threadIdx.x; int n = blockIdx.x; int patch = blockIdx.y; int d = tid; if (d >= D) return; __shared__ float patch_pixels[3 * 16 * 16]; // Load patch pixels cooperatively for (int i = tid; i < 768; i += blockDim.x) { int c = i / (p * p); int rem = i % (p * p); int h = rem / p; int w = rem % p; int patch_h = patch / (W / p); int patch_w = patch % (W / p); int img_idx = ((n * C + c) * H + (patch_h * p + h)) * W + (patch_w * p + w); patch_pixels[i] = img[img_idx]; } __syncthreads(); float sum = bias[d]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 768; i++) { sum += patch_pixels[i] * weight_t[i * D + d]; } sum += pos_emb[(patch + 1) * D + d]; out[n * (P + 1) * D + (patch + 1) * D + d] = sum; } ``` Now the weight access `weight_t[i * D + d]` for threads d=0..31 in a warp: - i*D + 0, i*D + 1, ..., i*D + 31 - These are consecutive floats! Fully coalesced! And `patch_pixels[i]` is the same for all threads in a warp, which is a broadcast and very efficient. This is a much better design. The only non-coalesced access is the initial image loading, which is coalesced because threads load consecutive pixels. This kernel should be quite fast! For the grid, I can launch (N, P) blocks. But CUDA limits grid dimensions. For N=2, P=196, grid is (2, 196). This is fine. Block size = 512 threads (for D=512). For a general D, I can use min(D, 512). But if D > 512, I need multiple blocks per patch. For simplicity, let's assume D <= 512 (which is true for test parameters). But to be general, I should handle arbitrary D. For arbitrary D, I could use: - 2D blocks: (min(D, 512), 1) - Grid: (N, P, ceil(D / 512)) With 3D grid: blockIdx.z handles the dim tile. Let me write the general version: ```cuda __global__ void vit_embed_kernel( const float* __restrict__ img, const float* __restrict__ weight_t, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ pos_emb, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C, int H, int W, int P, int D, int p ) { int tid = threadIdx.x; int n = blockIdx.x; int patch = blockIdx.y; int dim_tile = blockIdx.z; int d = dim_tile * blockDim.x + tid; if (d >= D) return; __shared__ float patch_pixels[768]; for (int i = tid; i < 768; i += blockDim.x) { int c = i / (p * p); int rem = i % (p * p); int h = rem / p; int w = rem % p; int patch_h = patch / (W / p); int patch_w = patch % (W / p); int img_idx = ((n * C + c) * H + (patch_h * p + h)) * W + (patch_w * p + w); patch_pixels[i] = img[img_idx]; } __syncthreads(); float sum = bias[d]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 768; i++) { sum += patch_pixels[i] * weight_t[i * D + d]; } sum += pos_emb[(patch + 1) * D + d]; out[n * (P + 1) * D + (patch + 1) * D + d] = sum; } ``` Grid: dim3(N, P, (D + 511) / 512) Block: 512 threads Wait, but 768 might not be the right patch_dim for general parameters. patch_dim = C * p * p. Can I fuse these into a custom kernel? Yes! I can write a kernel that takes conv2d output (N, D, Hp, Wp) and produces the final pre-transformer input (N, P+1, D) with cls token and pos emb. ```cuda __global__ void arrange_and_add_pos_emb( const float* __restrict__ conv_out, // (N, D, Hp, Wp) const float* __restrict__ pos_emb, // (1, P+1, D) const float* __restrict__ cls_token, // (1, 1, D) float* __restrict__ out, // (N, P+1, D) int N, int P, int D, int Hp, int Wp ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * (P + 1) * D; if (idx >= total) return; int d = idx % D; int tmp = idx / D; int s = tmp % (P + 1); int n = tmp / (P + 1); float val; if (s == 0) { val = cls_token[d] + pos_emb[d]; } else { int patch = s - 1; int patch_h = patch / Wp; int patch_w = patch % Wp; val = conv_out[((n * D + d) * Hp + patch_h) * Wp + patch_w] + pos_emb[s * D + d]; } out[idx] = val; } ``` This kernel is very simple and should be very fast. It saves: 1. The permute/flatten memory copy 2. The elementwise add memory copy 3. The concat memory copy And it uses Conv2d (cuDNN) for the heavy compute part. Is this better than the fully custom patch embedding kernel? - Conv2d: cuDNN optimized, likely very fast, maybe uses tensor cores - Custom kernel: saves one more memory roundtrip for conv output, but my custom kernel might not be as efficient as cuDNN I think the conv2d + custom arrangement kernel is probably faster because: 1. cuDNN is extremely well-optimized 2. The custom arrangement kernel is trivial (permutation + add) and very efficient 3. The conv2d output can stay in registers/cache while being rearranged Actually, wait. Grid: (N, P+1) Block: D threads ```cuda __global__ void vit_preprocess_kernel( const float* __restrict__ img, const float* __restrict__ weight_t, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ pos_emb, const float* __restrict__ cls_token, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C, int H, int W, int P, int D, int p ) { int tid = threadIdx.x; int n = blockIdx.x; int s = blockIdx.y; // 0 = cls, 1..P = patches int d = tid; if (d >= D) return; if (s == 0) { out[n * (P + 1) * D + d] = cls_token[d] + pos_emb[d]; } else { int patch = s - 1; __shared__ float patch_pixels[768]; for (int i = tid; i < 768; i += blockDim.x) { int c = i / (p * p); int rem = i % (p * p); int h = rem / p; int w = rem % p; int patch_h = patch / (W / p); int patch_w = patch % (W / p); int img_idx = ((n * C + c) * H + (patch_h * p + h)) * W + (patch_w * p + w); patch_pixels[i] = img[img_idx]; } __syncthreads(); float sum = bias[d]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 768; i++) { sum += patch_pixels[i] * weight_t[i * D + d]; } sum += pos_emb[s * D + d]; out[n * (P + 1) * D + s * D + d] = sum; } } ``` Grid: (N, P+1) Block: D threads (or min(D, 512)) For D=512, block size = 512. Now, let me write the actual kernel carefully. I need to calculate patch_h and patch_w correctly. For image size H and patch size p, number of patches vertically = H / p. For a patch index `patch` (0 to P-1): - patch_h = patch / (W / p) - patch_w = patch % (W / p) The top-left pixel of the patch is at (patch_h * p, patch_w * p). Let me verify with the test: H=W=224, p=16. W/p = 14. patch=0: patch_h=0, patch_w=0, top-left=(0,0) patch=1: patch_h=0, patch_w=1, top-left=(0,16) patch=13: patch_h=0, patch_w=13, top-left=(0,208) patch=14: patch_h=1, patch_w=0, top-left=(16,0) patch=195: patch_h=13, patch_w=13, top-left=(208,208) This matches the unfold behavior: unfold first over dim 2 (H), then dim 3 (W). In the reference: ```python x = img.unfold(2, p, p).unfold(3, p, p) ``` unfold(2, p, p) creates patches along H with stride p. unfold(3, p, p) creates patches along W with stride p. The result shape is (N, C, H/p, W/p, p, p). Then `.reshape(img.shape[0], -1, p*p*img.shape[1])` reshapes to (N, H/p * W/p, C*p*p). The ordering here: `.reshape` uses C-order, so the fastest-varying dimensions are the last ones: p, p, C. Wait, let me think. Original shape: (N, C, Hp, Wp, p, p) reshape to: (N, Hp*Wp, C*p*p) In C-order, the elements are arranged as: for n in N: for c in C: for hp in Hp: for wp in Wp: for h in p: for w in p: ... After reshape to (N, P, patch_dim): for n in N: for p_idx in P: for d in patch_dim: ... Since reshape preserves C-order, P = Hp * Wp in order: for hp in Hp, for wp in Wp. So patch 0 = hp=0, wp=0 patch 1 = hp=0, wp=1 ... patch Wp-1 = hp=0, wp=Wp-1 patch Wp = hp=1, wp=0 And patch_dim = C * p * p in order: for c in C, for h in p, for w in p. So pixel 0 = c=0, h=0, w=0 pixel 1 = c=0, h=0, w=1 ... pixel p-1 = c=0, h=0, w=p-1 pixel p = c=0, h=1, w=0 My kernel uses: ```cuda int c = i / (p * p); int rem = i % (p * p); int h = rem / p; int w = rem % p; ``` This gives i=0: c=0, h=0, w=0. i=1: c=0, h=0, w=1. i=p: c=0, h=1, w=0. This matches! And `patch_h = patch / Wp`, `patch_w = patch % Wp` gives the same ordering as reshape. So the kernel produces the same patch ordering. For the weight matrix: `self.patch_to_embedding.weight` has shape (D, patch_dim). In the linear layer, `output[d] = bias[d] + sum_i input[i] * weight[d, i]`. This is correct. Great, the kernel should be numerically equivalent. Let me write the complete kernel and Python code. That's fine. Wait, in my kernel design, I put the `if (s == 0)` before the `__syncthreads()`. If `s == 0`, all threads return before `__syncthreads()`. If `s > 0`, all threads execute the SMEM code and `__syncthreads()`. No divergence issues. Good. Now let me write the final code. Let me now write the complete code. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void vit_preprocess_kernel( const float* __restrict__ img, const float* __restrict__ weight_t, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ pos_emb, const float* __restrict__ cls_token, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C, int H, int W, int P, int D, int p ) { int tid = threadIdx.x; int n = blockIdx.x; int s = blockIdx.y; int dim_tile = blockIdx.z; int d = dim_tile * blockDim.x + tid; if (d >= D) return; if (s == 0) { out[n * (P + 1) * D + d] = cls_token[d] + pos_emb[d]; return; } int patch = s - 1; int patch_h = patch / (W / p); int patch_w = patch % (W / p); int ph = patch_h * p; int pw = patch_w * p; __shared__ float patch_pixels[768]; for (int i = tid; i < C * p * p; i += blockDim.x) { int c = i / (p * p); int rem = i % (p * p); int h = rem / p; int w = rem % p; int img_idx = ((n * C + c) * H + (ph + h)) * W + (pw + w); patch_pixels[i] = img[img_idx]; } __syncthreads(); float sum = bias[d]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < C * p * p; i++) { sum += patch_pixels[i] * weight_t[i * D + d]; } sum += pos_emb[s * D + d]; out[n * (P + 1) * D + s * D + d] = sum; } torch::Tensor vit_preprocess_cuda( torch::Tensor img, torch::Tensor weight_t, torch::Tensor bias, torch::Tensor pos_emb, torch::Tensor cls_token, int num_patches, int dim, int patch_size ) { int N = img.size(0); int C = img.size(1); int H = img.size(2); int W = img.size(3); int P = num_patches; int D = dim; int p = patch_size; auto out = torch::empty({N, P + 1, D}, img.options()); int threads = std::min(D, 1024); dim3 block(threads); dim3 grid(N, P + 1, (D + threads - 1) / threads); vit_preprocess_kernel<<<grid, block>>>( img.data_ptr<float>(), weight_t.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), pos_emb.data_ptr<float>(), cls_token.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), N, C, H, W, P, D, p ); return out; } """ cpp_ext = load_inline( name="vit_preprocess", cpp_sources=CUDA_SOURCE, functions=["vit_preprocess_cuda"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, image_size, patch_size, num_classes, dim, depth, heads, mlp_dim, channels=3, dropout=0.1, emb_dropout=0.1): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() assert image_size % patch_size == 0, "Image dimensions must be divisible by the patch size." num_patches = (image_size // patch_size) ** 2 patch_dim = channels * patch_size ** 2 self.patch_size = patch_size self.num_patches = num_patches self.dim = dim self.pos_embedding = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(1, num_patches + 1, dim)) self.patch_to_embedding = nn.Linear(patch_dim, dim) self.cls_token = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(1, 1, dim)) self.dropout = nn.Dropout(emb_dropout) self.transformer = nn.TransformerEncoder( nn.TransformerEncoderLayer(d_model=dim, nhead=heads, dim_feedforward=mlp_dim, dropout=dropout), num_layers=depth ) self.to_cls_token = nn.Identity() self.mlp_head = nn.Sequential( nn.Linear(dim, mlp_dim), nn.GELU(), nn.Dropout(dropout), nn.Linear(mlp_dim, num_classes) ) def forward(self, img): p = self.patch_size N = img.shape[0] P = self.num_patches D = self.dim weight_t = self.patch_to_embedding.weight.t().contiguous() bias = self.patch_to_embedding.bias.contiguous() x = cpp_ext.vit_preprocess_cuda( img, weight_t, bias, self.pos_embedding, self.cls_token, P, D, p ) x = self.dropout(x) x = self.transformer(x) x = self.to_cls_token(x[:, 0]) return self.mlp_head(x) ``` Wait, I need to store `image_size`, `channels`, `depth`, etc. if needed. Actually, `forward` only uses `p`, `N`, `P`, `D` and the layers. The constructor stores all the layers. One issue: `pos_embedding` and `cls_token` might be on CPU if `img` is on CPU. But the evaluation runs on CUDA. I'll assume everything is on the same device. Also, I should make sure the custom extension `load_inline` only runs once at module load time. Actually, on modern GPUs, as long as threads in a warp access consecutive addresses (even if split into groups), the memory requests are coalesced. Wait, this might actually be better: - Grid: (dim_tile, batch, 1) - Block: 196 threads (or 256 for better occupancy) - Each block computes 1 d value for all 196 patches in a batch - All threads in block read the same weight row (offset `d * patch_dim`) - Each thread reads one patch's pixels from the image For block size 256: - 196 threads are active, 60 are idle - Each active thread computes output for one patch and one d - Weight row is broadcast (all threads read the same address, or consecutive addresses within a small range) Actually, all threads read `weight[d * patch_dim + i]` at iteration i. Since d is the same for all threads in the block, they all read the same weight address at each iteration. Broadcast! Very efficient for weights! For image reads, each thread reads a different patch. The addresses might not be coalesced... but the image is small and already in L2 cache from previous blocks. For output writes, threads write to different patches but same d. The output layout is (N, P+1, D). So thread p writes to offset `(n * (P+1) + p + 1) * D + d`. For consecutive p values, the offsets differ by D = 512 floats = 2048 bytes. So consecutive threads write with a stride of 2048 bytes. Not fully coalesced, but the output is small anyway. Actually, this alternative design trades coalesced weight reads for broadcast weight reads, and coalesced image reads for scattered image reads. Since weight reads dominate memory bandwidth (1.5MB per d, read N*P times), making them broadcast is a big win. Wait, let's analyze properly. In the first design: - Per block: 1 patch, 512 d values - Weight reads per block: 512 rows * 768 floats = 393K floats - Image reads per block: 1 patch = 768 floats - Output writes per block: 512 floats In the second design: - Per block: 196 patches, 1 d value - Weight reads per block: 1 row * 768 floats = 768 floats (broadcast!) - Image reads per block: 196 patches = 196 * 768 = 150K floats - Output writes per block: 196 floats The second design reads 768 floats of weights per block vs 393K in the first design. That's a 500x reduction in weight memory traffic! But the image reads increase from 768 to 150K. And there are 512 blocks (one per d) vs 392 blocks (one per patch) in the first design. Wait, total weight reads: - First design: 392 blocks * 393K floats/block = 154M floats = 616MB of weight traffic - Second design: 512 blocks * 768 floats/block = 393K floats = 1.5MB of weight traffic! This is a massive reduction! The reason is that in the first design, each block reads the entire weight matrix. In the second design, each block reads only one row. And since there are only 512 rows total, the total weight traffic is just 512 * 768 = 393K floats = 1.5MB, which fits entirely in L1/L2 cache. But total image reads: - First design: 392 blocks * 768 floats/block = 300K floats = 1.2MB. But each block reads the SAME image data only for its n value. Since different blocks might share the same n and different patches, total image reads = N * P * 768 = 300K floats = 1.2MB. - Second design: 512 blocks * 196 patches * 768 floats/patch = wait, no. Each block handles ONE d value for ALL patches in one batch. So for N=2 and D=512: - Number of blocks = N * D = 2 * 512 = 1024 blocks - Each block reads all P patches for one n and one d: P * 768 = 196 * 768 = 150K floats - Total image reads = 1024 * 150K = 154M floats = 616MB Hmm, that's a lot of image reads. And each pixel is read D=512 times (once per d block). That's terrible! Wait, but in the first design, each pixel is also read multiple times. Let's re-examine: - First design: 392 blocks, each reads 768 pixels. Total pixel reads = 392 * 768 = 300K pixels. But there are only N * P = 392 patches total. Each patch pixel is read exactly once! Total = 392 * 768 = 300K pixels = 1.2MB. This is MINIMAL - each image pixel is read exactly once. - Second design: 1024 blocks, each reads all 392 patches = 392 * 768 pixels. Total pixel reads = 1024 * 392 * 768 = 308M pixels = 1.2GB of image reads. Each image pixel is read 512 times (once per d block). This is terrible! So the second design is much worse because it re-reads the image massively. What about a hybrid approach? - Each block handles K patches and K_d dims - K = 8 patches, K_d = 64 dims - Block size = 8 * 64 = 512 threads - SMEM: 8 patches * 768 pixels = 6K floats = 24KB - Each block reads 8 * 768 = 6K pixels from image - Each block reads 64 * 768 = 49K weight values - Total blocks = N * (P/8) * (D/64) = 2 * 25 * 8 = 400 blocks - Total image reads = 400 * 6K = 2.4M pixels. But there are only 300K unique pixels. So each pixel is read 8 times (once per dim tile of 64). This is because the same 8 patches are loaded by each dim tile block. - Total weight reads = 400 * 49K = 19.6M weight values. But there are only 512 * 768 = 393K unique weight values. So each weight is read 50 times. The first design (1 patch, 512 dims) reads each image pixel once and each weight value 392 times (once per patch block). Total: - Image: 300K pixels = 1.2MB - Weight: 392 * 393K = 154M values = 616MB The hybrid (8 patches, 64 dims) reads each image pixel 8 times and each weight value 50 times. Total: - Image: 2.4M pixels = 9.6MB - Weight: 19.6M values = 78MB The hybrid has lower total memory traffic than the first design (88MB vs 617MB) but higher than optimal. However, the image pixels and weights are cached after the first read. Since the image is 1.2MB and weights are 1.5MB, both fit in L2 cache. So repeated reads are L2 cache hits. In the first design, the weight reads go from L2 but there are many of them (616MB of weight traffic at L2 bandwidth). In the hybrid, weight reads are 78MB of L2 traffic. The hybrid should be faster if L2 bandwidth is a bottleneck. But the hybrid is more complex to implement. The first design, with 616MB of L2 traffic, might still be very fast. H100 L2 bandwidth is extremely high (probably 10-20 TB/s). At 10 TB/s, 616MB takes 61 microseconds. At 20 TB/s, 31 microseconds. The compute is 308M FLOPs. At 67 TFLOPS, 4.6 microseconds. So the kernel is likely memory-bound. If I use the hybrid, 88MB at 20 TB/s = 4.4 microseconds + compute 4.6 microseconds = 9 microseconds. That's roughly 3x faster. But wait, the hybrid has lower parallelism: 400 blocks vs 392 blocks. Actually they're similar in block count. But each block does more work. This is getting complicated. Let me just go with the first design and see if it's fast enough. I can always iterate. Actually, I just realized: in the first design, consecutive blocks (same n, consecutive patch) read the SAME weight matrix but the weight matrix is only 1.5MB. Once the first block loads it into L2, all subsequent blocks get L2 hits. So the effective bandwidth is L2 bandwidth, which is much higher than DRAM bandwidth. L2 bandwidth on H100 is around 10-15 TB/s (I think). At 10 TB/s, 616MB takes ~60 microseconds. But the actual time is less because blocks run in parallel and share the L2 cache. The first block from each SM reads weights from DRAM into L2. Then subsequent blocks on that SM read from L2. With 132 SMs on H100 and 392 blocks, some SMs get 3 blocks, some get 2. The total weight traffic per SM is ~3 * 1.5MB = 4.5MB. At L2 bandwidth, this is very fast. I think the first design should work fine and is much simpler to get right. OK final decision: go with the first design. Let me write the code. One more thing: I should consider whether to use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` at module load time or delay it. Module load time is fine. Let me write the complete code now.

+ 1) * D + s * D + d + 2] = sum2 + pos.z;\n if (d + 3 < D) out[n * (P + 1) * D + s * D + d + 3] = sum3 + pos.w;\n }\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor vit_preprocess_cuda(\n torch::Tensor img,\n torch::Tensor float4 pos = *(const float4*)(pos_emb + s * D + d);\n out[n * (P + 1) * D + s * D + d] = sum0 + pos.x;\n if (d + 1 < D) out[n * (P + 1) * D + s * D + d + 1] = sum1 + pos.y;\n if (d + 2 < D) out[n * (P + 1) * D + s * D + d + 2] = sum2 + pos.z;\n if (d + 3 < D) out[n * (P + 1) * D + s * D + d + 3] = sum3 + pos.w;\n }\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor vit_preprocess_cuda(\n torch::Tensor img,\n torch::Tensor weight_t,\n torch::Tensor bias,\n torch::Tensor pos_emb,\n torch::Tensor cls_token,\n int num_patches,\n int dim,\n int patch_size\n) {\n int N = img.size(0);\n int C = img.size(1);\n int H = img.size(2);\n int W = img.size(3);\n int P = num_patches;\n int D = dim;\n int p = patch_size;\n int patch_dim = C * p * p;\n \n auto out = torch::empty({N, P + 1, D}, img.options());\n \n int \"--use_fast_math\"],\n verbose=False,\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, image_size, patch_size, num_classes, dim, depth, heads, mlp_dim, channels=3, dropout=0.1, emb_dropout=0.1):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n \n assert image_size % patch_size == 0, \"Image dimensions must be divisible by the patch size.\"\n num_patches = (image_size // patch_size) ** 2\n patch_dim = channels * patch_size ** 2\n \n self.patch_size = patch_size\n self.num_patches = num_patches\n self.dim = dim\n self.depth = depth\n self.heads = heads\n self.mlp_dim = mlp_dim\n self.pos_embedding = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(1, num_patches + 1, dim))\n self.patch_to_embedding = nn.Linear(patch_dim, dim)\n self.cls_token = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(1, 1, dim))\n self.dropout = self.cls_token[0, 0],\n P, D, p\n )\n \n x = self.dropout(x)\n \n for layer in self.layers:\n x = layer(x)\n \n x = self.norm(x)\n x = x[:, 0]\n return self.mlp_head(x)\n\nclass self.proj = nn.Linear(dim, dim)\n self.dropout = nn.Dropout(dropout)\n \n def forward(self, x):\n B, N, C = x.shape\n qkv = self.qkv(x).reshape(B, N, 3, self.heads, C // self.heads).permute(2, 0, 3, 1, 4)\n q, k, v = qkv[0], qkv[1], qkv[2]\n \n attn = F.scaled_dot_product_attention(q, k, v, dropout_p=0.0, is_causal=False)\n \n x = attn.transpose(1, 2).reshape(B, N, C)\n x = self.proj(x)\n x = self.dropout(x)\n return x\n\nclass MLP(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, dim, hidden_dim, dropout):\n super().__init__()\n self.net = nn.Sequential(\n nn.Linear(dim, hidden_dim),\n nn.GELU(),\n reasoning
The user is reminding me that I have 2 turns and 18 tool calls remaining. I just compiled a version with a manual transformer implementation, but I should step back and think about what will actually be fastest. The key insight is that the PyTorch reference uses `nn.TransformerEncoder` with default settings. The heavy compute is in the transformer layers (attention + MLP), not the preprocessing. My preprocessing kernel is already very fast. For the transformer, the best approach to get speedup over the reference is likely to use `F.scaled_dot_product_attention` (which uses Flash Attention on H100) inside manually-written transformer blocks, rather than `nn.TransformerEncoder` which might not use the optimal attention implementation. Let me run correctness on my latest version to see if it's correct, then decide whether to submit.

import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

cpp_sources = """
#include <torch/extension.h>
torch::Tensor vit_preprocess_cuda(
    torch::Tensor img,
    torch::Tensor weight_t,
    torch::Tensor bias,
    torch::Tensor pos_emb,
    torch::Tensor cls_token,
    int num_patches,
    int dim,
    int patch_size
);
"""

cuda_sources = r"""
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <cuda_runtime.h>

__global__ void vit_preprocess_kernel(
    const float* __restrict__ img,
    const float* __restrict__ weight_t,
    const float* __restrict__ bias,
    const float* __restrict__ pos_emb,
    const float* __restrict__ cls_token,
    float* __restrict__ out,
    int N, int C, int H, int W, int P, int D, int p
) {
    int tid = threadIdx.x;
    int n = blockIdx.x;
    int s = blockIdx.y;
    
    if (s == 0) {
        for (int d = tid; d < D; d += blockDim.x) {
            out[n * (P + 1) * D + d] = cls_token[d] + pos_emb[d];
        }
        return;
    }
    
    int patch = s - 1;
    int patch_h = patch / (W / p);
    int patch_w = patch % (W / p);
    int ph = patch_h * p;
    int pw = patch_w * p;
    int patch_dim = C * p * p;
    
    extern __shared__ float s_patch_pixels[];
    
    for (int i = tid; i < patch_dim; i += blockDim.x) {
        int c = i / (p * p);
        int rem = i % (p * p);
        int h = rem / p;
        int w = rem % p;
        int img_idx = ((n * C + c) * H + (ph + h)) * W + (pw + w);
        s_patch_pixels[i] = img[img_idx];
    }
    __syncthreads();
    
    int D_VEC = 4;
    for (int d = tid * D_VEC; d < D; d += blockDim.x * D_VEC) {
        float sum0 = bias[d];
        float sum1 = (d + 1 < D) ? bias[d + 1] : 0.0f;
        float sum2 = (d + 2 < D) ? bias[d + 2] : 0.0f;
        float sum3 = (d + 3 < D) ? bias[d + 3] : 0.0f;
        
        for (int i = 0; i < patch_dim; i++) {
            float pix = s_patch_pixels[i];
            float4 w = *(const float4*)(weight_t + i * D + d);
            sum0 += pix * w.x;
            sum1 += pix * w.y;
            sum2 += pix * w.z;
            sum3 += pix * w.w;
        }
        
        float4 pos = *(const float4*)(pos_emb + s * D + d);
        out[n * (P + 1) * D + s * D + d]     = sum0 + pos.x;
        if (d + 1 < D) out[n * (P + 1) * D + s * D + d + 1] = sum1 + pos.y;
        if (d + 2 < D) out[n * (P + 1) * D + s * D + d + 2] = sum2 + pos.z;
        if (d + 3 < D) out[n * (P + 1) * D + s * D + d + 3] = sum3 + pos.w;
    }
}

torch::Tensor vit_preprocess_cuda(
    torch::Tensor img,
    torch::Tensor weight_t,
    torch::Tensor bias,
    torch::Tensor pos_emb,
    torch::Tensor cls_token,
    int num_patches,
    int dim,
    int patch_size
) {
    int N = img.size(0);
    int C = img.size(1);
    int H = img.size(2);
    int W = img.size(3);
    int P = num_patches;
    int D = dim;
    int p = patch_size;
    int patch_dim = C * p * p;
    
    auto out = torch::empty({N, P + 1, D}, img.options());
    
    int threads = 128;
    dim3 block(threads);
    dim3 grid(N, P + 1);
    size_t smem_size = patch_dim * sizeof(float);
    
    vit_preprocess_kernel<<<grid, block, smem_size>>>(
        img.data_ptr<float>(),
        weight_t.data_ptr<float>(),
        bias.data_ptr<float>(),
        pos_emb.data_ptr<float>(),
        cls_token.data_ptr<float>(),
        out.data_ptr<float>(),
        N, C, H, W, P, D, p
    );
    
    return out;
}
"""

cpp_ext = load_inline(
    name="vit_preprocess_final",
    cpp_sources=cpp_sources,
    cuda_sources=cuda_sources,
    functions=["vit_preprocess_cuda"],
    extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"],
    verbose=False,
)

class ModelNew(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, image_size, patch_size, num_classes, dim, depth, heads, mlp_dim, channels=3, dropout=0.1, emb_dropout=0.1):
        super(ModelNew, self).__init__()
        
        assert image_size % patch_size == 0, "Image dimensions must be divisible by the patch size."
        num_patches = (image_size // patch_size) ** 2
        patch_dim = channels * patch_size ** 2
        
        self.patch_size = patch_size
        self.num_patches = num_patches
        self.dim = dim
        self.pos_embedding = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(1, num_patches + 1, dim))
        self.patch_to_embedding = nn.Linear(patch_dim, dim)
        self.cls_token = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(1, 1, dim))
        self.dropout = nn.Dropout(emb_dropout)
        
        self.transformer = nn.TransformerEncoder(
            nn.TransformerEncoderLayer(d_model=dim, nhead=heads, dim_feedforward=mlp_dim, dropout=dropout, batch_first=True),
            num_layers=depth
        )
        
        self.to_cls_token = nn.Identity()
        self.mlp_head = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Linear(dim, mlp_dim),
            nn.GELU(),
            nn.Dropout(dropout),
            nn.Linear(mlp_dim, num_classes)
        )
    
    def forward(self, img):
        p = self.patch_size
        N = img.shape[0]
        P = self.num_patches
        D = self.dim
        
        weight_t = self.patch_to_embedding.weight.t().contiguous()
        bias = self.patch_to_embedding.bias.contiguous()
        
        x = cpp_ext.vit_preprocess_cuda(
            img, weight_t, bias,
            self.pos_embedding[0], self.cls_token[0, 0],
            P, D, p
        )
        
        x = self.dropout(x)
        x = self.transformer(x)
        x = self.to_cls_token(x[:, 0])
        return self.mlp_head(x)
